import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, num_classes=1000):
        """
        MobileNetV2 architecture implementation in PyTorch.

        :param num_classes: The number of output classes. Default is 1000.
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        
        def _make_divisible(v, divisor, min_value=None):
            """
            This function ensures that the number of channels is divisible by the divisor.
            """
            if min_value is None:
                min_value = divisor
            new_v = max(min_value, int(v + divisor / 2) // divisor * divisor)
            # Make sure that round down does not go down by more than 10%.
            if new_v < 0.9 * v:
                new_v += divisor
            return new_v

        def _inverted_residual_block(inp, oup, stride, expand_ratio):
            """
            Inverted Residual Block for MobileNetV2.
            """
            hidden_dim = int(inp * expand_ratio)
            use_res_connect = stride == 1 and inp == oup

            layers = []
            if expand_ratio != 1:
                # Pointwise convolution
                layers.append(nn.Conv2d(inp, hidden_dim, 1, 1, 0, bias=False))
                layers.append(nn.BatchNorm2d(hidden_dim))
                layers.append(nn.ReLU6(inplace=True))

            layers.extend([
                # Depthwise convolution
                nn.Conv2d(hidden_dim, hidden_dim, 3, stride, 1, groups=hidden_dim, bias=False),
                nn.BatchNorm2d(hidden_dim),
                nn.ReLU6(inplace=True),
                # Pointwise linear convolution
                nn.Conv2d(hidden_dim, oup, 1, 1, 0, bias=False),
                nn.BatchNorm2d(oup),
            ])

            if use_res_connect:
                return nn.Sequential(*layers), True
            else:
                return nn.Sequential(*layers), False

        # MobileNetV2 architecture
        input_channel = 32
        last_channel = 1280
        inverted_residual_setting = [
            # t, c, n, s
            [1, 16, 1, 1],
            [6, 24, 2, 2],
            [6, 32, 3, 2],
            [6, 64, 4, 2],
            [6, 96, 3, 1],
            [6, 160, 3, 2],
            [6, 320, 1, 1],
        ]

        # Building first layer
        features = [nn.Conv2d(3, input_channel, 3, 2, 1, bias=False),
                    nn.BatchNorm2d(input_channel),
                    nn.ReLU6(inplace=True)]

        # Building inverted residual blocks
        for t, c, n, s in inverted_residual_setting:
            output_channel = _make_divisible(c, 8)
            for i in range(n):
                stride = s if i == 0 else 1
                features.append(_inverted_residual_block(input_channel, output_channel, stride, expand_ratio=t)[0])
                input_channel = output_channel

        # Building last several layers
        features.append(nn.Conv2d(input_channel, last_channel, 1, 1, 0, bias=False))
        features.append(nn.BatchNorm2d(last_channel))
        features.append(nn.ReLU6(inplace=True))

        # Final layer
        features.append(nn.AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1, 1)))

        self.features = nn.Sequential(*features)

        # Linear layer
        self.classifier = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Dropout(0.0),
            nn.Linear(last_channel, num_classes),
        )

        # Weight initialization
        for m in self.modules():
            if isinstance(m, nn.Conv2d):
                nn.init.kaiming_normal_(m.weight, mode='fan_out')
                if m.bias is not None:
                    nn.init.zeros_(m.bias)
            elif isinstance(m, nn.BatchNorm2d):
                nn.init.ones_(m.weight)
                nn.init.zeros_(m.bias)
            elif isinstance(m, nn.Linear):
                nn.init.normal_(m.weight, 0, 0.01)
                nn.init.zeros_(m.bias)

    def forward(self, x):
        """
        Forward pass of the MobileNetV2 model.

        :param x: The input tensor, shape (batch_size, 3, 224, 224)
        :return: The output tensor, shape (batch_size, num_classes)
        """
        x = self.features(x)
        x = x.view(x.size(0), -1)
        x = self.classifier(x)
        return x

# Test code
batch_size = 10
num_classes = 1000

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, 3, 224, 224)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [num_classes]

So moving along channel dimension is strided by H*W. Thus for a 1x1 conv, each output channel is computed by dot product of weights with input across channels with stride H*W. But storing ref might also store all the modules, which is fine. This approach lets us avoid writing initialization code. However, we need to make sure we don't accidentally call `self.ref.forward()` inside `forward` (that would be the reference). The task tests `ModelNew` independently. Let's think about the custom kernel design. We'll have a C++ host function `mobilenetv2_forward` that takes input Tensor and a list/struct of weight tensors, and returns output Tensor. Passing a huge number of weight tensors as arguments to a C++ function via pybind is cumbersome but possible. We can pass them as a vector of Tensor. In C++, we iterate over the layers and call per-layer kernels. But we need to write CUDA kernels for each operation. Let's prototype the kernels: 1. `conv1x1_bn_relu6_forward`: input [N, C_in, H, W], weight [C_out, C_in], bias [C_out] (fused weight and bias). Output [N, C_out, H, W]. Applies ReLU6 if relu6=True. Naive kernel: each thread computes one output element out[n,c_out,h,w]. Grid: (N, H, W) blocks of (C_out / blocksize) threads? Or each block handles a spatial tile and threads iterate over output channels. Better: blockDim.x = 128. Grid: (N*H*W). Each block processes one spatial pixel (or a few) and computes all output channels. But N*H*W can be up to 10*112*112=125k. With blocks of 128 threads, that's enough occupancy. Wait, if each block corresponds to one spatial pixel, then each thread computes a subset of output channels. That's not ideal because C_out up to 1280, which is small. So each block computing 1280 channels with 128 threads => each thread computes 10 channels. This is fine. But if we have one block per pixel, grid size = 125k blocks, which is fine for GPU. Alternatively, we can have each block handle a tile of e.g., 4x4 spatial pixels and each thread handle a channel. But simpler: one block per pixel, threads cooperative load weights? Actually weights [C_out, C_in] are shared across all pixels, and each pixel reads [C_in] elements. There is no reuse across pixels in a block. So might as well have each thread compute its own subset of channels. But we need memory coalescing: we want adjacent threads to access adjacent output elements. Since output is NCHW, the channel dimension is the second outermost. For a given n,h,w, channels are contiguous in memory? No: NCHW -> stride for channel = H*W. So for fixed n,h,w, out[n,c,h,w] = base + c*H*W. So adjacent channels are strided by H*W, not contiguous. So adjacent threads accessing adjacent channels simultaneously would result in strided access with stride H*W*sizeof(float). If H*W=12544, that's a huge stride, leading to poor memory coalescing. That's bad. Better to assign each thread a contiguous chunk of channels? No, that makes adjacent threads access elements far apart. In CUDA, memory is fetched in 128-byte cache lines. Strided access of 12544 floats means each thread touches a different cache line, which leads to maximum bandwidth. Thus a naive element-wise kernel for 1x1 conv on NCHW is not ideal. We should think about transposition or tile-based access. To improve coalescing for the output, we can assign a warp to compute multiple spatial pixels for a *single* output channel. For example, each warp (32 threads) computes 32 spatial pixels (or a tile) for one output channel. Then adjacent threads access adjacent spatial positions, which are contiguous in memory. Each thread loads the same weight (weight for that output channel across all input channels) and loads input pixels. This uses shared memory or broadcasts for weights. Since weights are common for all pixels in the block, they can be loaded into shared memory once per block. This is much better. Let's design: For a given output channel `c`, we want to compute `out[n,c,h,w] = sum_{ic} in[n,ic,h,w] * w[c,ic] + b[c]`. We can process multiple spatial pixels in parallel and reduce over input channels. This is analogous to a matrix multiplication: A = input reshaped as [N*H*W, C_in], B = weight^T [C_in, C_out], output = A * B. In NCHW, A is not row-major over spatial. If we treat spatial as rows, then within a row (one pixel), the elements (channels) are strided by H*W. So A is stored with a huge leading dimension. Not great for direct GEMM. If we transpose input to [N, C_in, H*W] contiguous on the last dim? But that requires a transpose per layer, which costs memory bandwidth. Might still be worth it if we can use CUBLAS. Transpose + CUBLAS might be faster than strided naive. Another approach: since H*W <= 12544 and C_in up to 960, maybe transposing is cheap. Actually, CuBLAS can handle transpose operations. For C = op(A) * op(B). If we set A as (C_in) x (N*H*W) and B as C_in x C_out, then we get C as C_out x (N*H*W) which is contiguous in spatial dimension if we treat as NCHW? Let's see. If we treat input as a matrix of size C_in x (N*H*W) where the leading dimension is N*H*W (row-major). Then element (ic, row_idx) corresponds to in[n,ic,h,w] where row_idx enumerates n,h,w. In memory NCHW, the element in[n,ic,h,w] is at offset n*C*H*W + ic*H*W + h*W + w. So if we fix ic, the spatial elements are contiguous (since they span h*W+w). Great! So if we treat each channel as a row of length N*H*W, the row is contiguous (since H*W contiguous, and channels are far apart; but wait N dimension across channels: after channel ic finishes H*W elements, next channel starts. But N dimension is outermost: channel ic contains all N*H*W elements? No: NCHW means the innermost is W, then H, then C, then N. So the memory layout is: offset = ((n * C + ic) * H + h) * W + w. For a fixed n and ic, varying h,w is contiguous (H*W block). But if n changes, the next block is at offset ( (n+1)*C + ic )*H*W. So the elements for a fixed ic across all n are not contiguous; they separated by (C-1)*H*W. So treating a channel across all N as a contiguous vector only works if N==1. If N=10, each channel's data consists of 10 blocks of H*W elements, separated by other channels. So the stride between n blocks is C*H*W. So the memory layout for a channel is: [block_n0 (H*W contiguous), gap, block_n1, gap, ...]. So it's not fully contiguous, but it has regular strides. CuBLAS supports strided batched GEMM, but this pattern is not exactly batched. However, if we reorder to NHWC (channels last), then pointwise conv becomes a GEMM where spatial pixels are rows and channels are columns, contiguous for each pixel. That's ideal. cuDNN uses NHWC in newer versions. In PyTorch, NCHW is default. We could convert input to NHWC once at the beginning. The first conv (3x3) could be easier in NHWC too. But the weights in PyTorch are NCHW (out,in,k,k). NHWC weights would be (out,H,W,in)? Actually 1x1 conv weight is (out,in,1,1). In NHWC, we might want weight shape (out,in) as well. But rewriting everything in NHWC is a huge change to weight extraction. Let's revisit the coalescing issue. For a spatial pixel, output channels are not contiguous, but memory is contiguous across W, H, N, then C. So for a fixed n,h,w, reading all input channels in[] gives strided reads by H*W. For 112x112, stride=12544 floats = ~50KB. That's larger than a cache line, so each channel read goes to a different cache line. For C_in=32, reading 32 cache lines per pixel is ~2KB. With many threads, the L2 cache might struggle. But maybe we can reorder the loops: process a block of channels for a block of spatial pixels. For example, a thread block of (32,8) where x-dim is spatial (W) and y-dim is channel. But channels are strided heavily, so coalescing within a warp would require that adjacent threads in x-dim access adjacent W (contiguous). That's good. So we can have each warp compute 32 output channels for 8 spatial positions? Let's see: if we have 32 threads reading adjacent W for a fixed n,h and different out_channel, they don't read adjacent memory. Actually, to get coalesced reads of input, we want threads in a warp to read adjacent spatial positions, because those are contiguous. So perhaps each thread computes a single output channel for multiple spatial positions in sequence. Or a warp computes a single output channel for 32 adjacent spatial positions (e.g., along W). Then 32 threads read 32 contiguous input pixels for each input channel in a loop. This is excellent coalescing: reading input[n,ic,h,w0..w31] is contiguous. Each thread computes `sum_ic in * weight`. After the loop, each thread has the dot product for one pixel. Then apply bias, relu6, write to output[n,oc,h,w]. But writing output: for a fixed oc, adjacent w are contiguous. So if each thread in warp writes to adjacent w, writes are coalesced. Great. For oc, we can have each block process a tile of output channels (e.g., 8 or 16) and a tile of spatial rows/cols? Each thread in block can handle a specific oc and a specific spatial coordinate. But the dot product over ic needs to load weight[oc,ic] for each ic. For a fixed oc, all threads in warp for spatial positions use the same weight. So we can put weight in shared memory or keep it in registers and broadcast via constant cache. With 32 threads, broadcasting weight per ic is okay: all threads read the same address, which gets cached. Similarly for reading input across threads for different w. So the algorithm: For each oc in block's output channel range: acc = 0 for ic in 0..C_in-1: w = weight[oc, ic] load input[n, ic, h, w + threadIdx.x] for all w in tile acc += in * w write output. However each thread also needs a specific spatial position (n,h,w). If each warp handles 32 adjacent w for one (n,h), then grid can be (n, h, oc_block, w_block). However, to match the reference numerically, we need to apply the same operations in the same order. Since we fuse conv+bn, and compute in float32, the result might be slightly different due to reordering of operations (e.g., fused conv+bn vs separate conv then bn). The tolerance is 1e-4 absolute/relative. If we do standard fusion (fold BN into conv weight and bias), the result is mathematically equivalent in real arithmetic, but floating point rounding can differ slightly. However, weight folding fusion is essentially: y = (conv(x) - mean) / sqrt(var+eps) * gamma + beta = conv(x) * (gamma / sqrt(var+eps)) + (beta - mean * gamma / sqrt(var+eps)). When we compute conv(x) with modified weights, we get rounding differences in weights but the computation order is conv with modified weights + bias, which is not exactly the same as conv(x) in original weights then BN, but the difference should be within 1e-4 for typical weight magnitudes? Probably yes. However, cross-correlation of large sums can have small differences. But 1e-4 is fairly loose for fp32. Also, because PyTorch uses cuDNN conv which might use different algorithms (Winograd, implicit GEMM), the intermediate results might already differ from a custom direct conv kernel by more than 1e-4? Typically cuDNN algorithms are deterministic and match direct conv within ~1e-5 for fp32. But our custom kernel does direct conv slightly differently. With tolerance 1e-4, it's probably okay. Let's design the fused weights extraction. For each Conv2d + BN pair (followed optionally by ReLU6), we want to compute: ``` w_fused = conv.weight * (bn.weight / sqrt(bn.running_var + bn.eps)).view(-1,1,1,1) b_fused = (bn.bias - bn.running_mean * bn.weight / sqrt(bn.running_var + bn.eps)) ``` Note: for depthwise conv, weight shape is [C,1,3,3]. The multiplication with gamma/std needs broadcasting correctly. In PyTorch: ``` std = torch.sqrt(bn.running_var + bn.eps) w = conv.weight * (bn.weight / std).view(-1, 1, 1, 1) b = bn.bias - bn.running_mean * bn.weight / std ``` For Conv2d without bias, this works. For layers where there is no BN after (e.g., after the last pointwise in a block there is BN, so all convs have BN). In this model, every conv is followed by BN. The only exception is the final linear layer, which has no BN. Also, ReLU6 is applied after BN for certain layers. For layers where ReLU6 follows BN, our kernel will compute fused conv+bias and then apply relu6: `out = min(max(x + b, 0), 6)` (actually after weight fusion, the conv output includes scaling, then add bias). Wait, correction: y = conv(x, fused_weight) + fused_bias. Then if relu6=True, y = min(max(y, 0), 6). For pointwise conv (1x1), we can flatten weight to [C_out, C_in]. Let's think about the implementation strategy. Since there are many layers, we could write a Python fusing routine that iterates over `ref.features` and fuses each conv+bn(+relu6). Then stores arrays of fused weights and biases, plus metadata (stride, groups, kernel_size, use_relu6). We then pass these arrays to the C++ extension. But because CUDA kernels need to know dimensions at compile time or runtime, passing a struct of Tensor + int parameters per layer is doable. However, implementing 30+ layers in C++ manually by calling kernels is error-prone. We can write a generic `sequential_forward` that loops over layers but each layer is a kernel call. Let's define a struct for layer params: ```cpp struct LayerParam { at::Tensor weight; at::Tensor bias; int kernel_size; int stride; int padding; int groups; bool relu6; }; ``` But passing a vector of `LayerParam` from Python to pybind is possible if we define an opaque type, but it's easier to just pass separate lists of tensors and integers. Or we can create a Python list of dicts and in the C++ wrapper unpack them. But the pybind wrapping via `load_inline` is generated automatically from `functions=[...]`. We can define one function that takes the input and a bunch of tensors. Since we have many layers, having a function with ~60 tensor arguments is not practical. Better: we can pack all weights and biases into a list of `torch.Tensor` in Python, and the C++ function can accept `std::vector<at::Tensor> weights`, `std::vector<at::Tensor> biases`, and `std::vector<int>` metadata. But `std::vector<at::Tensor>` is supported by pybind11 and ATen. Let's design the Python side: - In `__init__`, build the reference `Model(num_classes)`. - Iterate over `ref.features` sequentially. For each `nn.Conv2d`, the next layer is `nn.BatchNorm2d`, and the next may be `nn.ReLU6`. - Fuse them: modify conv weight, create bias tensor, skip bn and relu6 layers. - Collect fused weights and biases into Python lists. Also collect metadata (stride, padding, groups, kernel_size, use_relu6, H_out, W_out, C_in, C_out). - We can also precompute output shapes for each layer. - Register weights and biases as parameters (or buffers) of ModelNew so they persist. For simplicity, we can store them in `self.params` which is a nn.ParameterList or just a list. Since they are derived from ref model parameters, we need to ensure they are on the correct device. `nn.ParameterList` registers them as parameters. Actually, since we won't be computing gradients (forward only), registering as buffers or parameters is fine. But if we use `nn.ParameterList`, they are tracked. Then in `forward`, call the custom extension function, passing the input and the list of weights/biases and metadata. The C++ function signature: ```cpp at::Tensor mobilenetv2_forward( at::Tensor x, std::vector<at::Tensor> weights, std::vector<at::Tensor> biases, std::vector<int64_t> strides, std::vector<int64_t> paddings, std::vector<int64_t> groups, std::vector<int64_t> kernel_sizes, std::vector<int64_t> relu6_flags, at::Tensor linear_weight, at::Tensor linear_bias) { ... } ``` Wait, we also need to handle the initial 3x3 conv. Its kernel size is 3, the rest are either 1 or 3. We need a generic kernel for 3x3 conv (with arbitrary groups and stride). For pointwise 1x1, we use a specialized kernel. For depthwise 3x3 (groups=C), we can also use a specialized kernel that is more efficient, but a generic 3x3 kernel can handle it too. Given time, maybe we can just write one generic conv2d kernel for any k=1 or 3. For k=1, the generic kernel reduces to a simpler pattern, but still loops over kh,kw (which is 1). That might be okay. However, for pointwise conv with C_in=960 and H=W=7, each output pixel does 960 multiply-adds. Looping over kh,kw (size 1) is fine. So maybe we only need two kernels: `conv2d_generic` for 1x1 and 3x3 (with arbitrary groups), and `depthwise_conv3x3` if we want to optimize. But the generic 3x3 kernel for group=1 with C_in=3 is tiny, no problem. For depthwise, groups=C and C_in=C_out, it's also fine with a generic kernel? In a generic conv kernel, each output element loops over all input channels divided by groups, and all kernel positions. For depthwise with groups=C, the number of input channels per group is 1. So each output pixel does 9 multiply-adds. That's trivial. But the memory access pattern might be okay. However, generic conv kernel with groups > 1 requires indexing into weight and input correctly. The formula for grouped conv: for output channel oc, group = oc / (C_out / groups), and it reads input channels from group * (C_in / groups) to (group+1)*(C_in/groups). For depthwise, C_in/groups = 1. Let's write a generic `conv2d_forward_kernel` that handles arbitrary kernel size, stride, padding, groups. Since all our layers are k=1 or 3, stride=1 or 2, padding=0 or 1. But padding can be determined: for 3x3 with stride=1, pad=1; stride=2, pad=1; for 1x1, pad=0. We can compute H_out, W_out from the metadata. The generic kernel: ``` __global__ void conv2d_forward_kernel( const float* in, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* out, int N, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out, int H_out, int W_out, int k, int stride, int pad, int groups, bool relu6) { ``` Each thread computes one output element (n, oc, h, w). Grid: blocks along spatial and channel dims. As before, use 3D grid: blockIdx.x = w_block (tile for W_out) blockIdx.y = h blockIdx.z = n*C_out + oc blockDim.x = 32 Then each thread computes for a specific w_out and loops over kh,kw and ic_per_group. Let's calculate: total threads per block = 32. We can compute one output pixel per thread. To have enough parallelism, grid size = N*C_out*H_out*ceil(W_out/32). For largest C_out=1280, H=7, W=7, N=10: grid = 10*1280*7*1 = 89600 blocks, each 32 threads = 2.8M threads, which is enough. Coalescing: if blockIdx.x corresponds to w_out/32, then adjacent threads in x have consecutive w_out. Writing to out[n,oc,h,w_out] are contiguous in memory (since W is innermost). Reading input for a given ic and kh,kw: in[n, ic, h*stride + kh - pad, w_out*stride + kw - pad] . Here w_out varies across threads, so the column index varies, and the memory is contiguous (since W is innermost). Excellent! So reads are coalesced. For weight reads: weight[oc, ic, kh, kw] is the same for all threads in block (since oc and kh,kw,ic are fixed in inner loops). Broadcast. Good. This generic kernel with `blockDim.x=32` and grid as above is actually quite efficient, especially for pointwise conv (k=1) where the inner loops are over ic. Even for k=3, inner loop is 3x3*ic_per_group. For group=1 and C_in=3, it's 27 iterations. For group=96 and C_in=96, ic_per_group=1, so 9 iterations. Very small. The only potential inefficiency is that for pointwise conv with large C_in (e.g., 960), each thread does 960 iterations. But since it's entirely memory-bound and reads are coalesced, it should be decent. With 32 threads per block, each block does 32*960 = 30,720 multiply-adds, which is okay. Actually, for pointwise with C_in large and C_out large, we might want to have each block compute a tile of output channels to improve weight reuse via L1 cache. But even with our design, if many blocks for different oc are running concurrently on the same SM, they share the same ic input reads (since ic loops are similar). The L2 cache will hold the input for the current ic across all blocks. This is okay. Let's compute occupancy: each thread has a few registers (accumulator, loop variables), maybe ~20-30. With block size 32, that's 32 threads per block. Occupancy might be low per SM because each block is small. We might want block size larger, e.g., 128, and have each thread handle 4 consecutive w_out or something. But that complicates the kernel. Let's keep block size = 32 or 64. Actually 32 threads per block is too few for an SM (warps per SM is limited). Block size of 32 means one warp per block. An SM can have up to 64 warps (on Ampere). But the launch overhead is higher with many blocks. Let's choose blockDim.x = 128 (4 warps). That means each block processes 4 consecutive w_out positions. Wait, we want coalescing, so we can have each thread handle one w_out. Block size 128 gives 128 threads per block, which is better occupancy. If W_out is not a multiple of 128, we handle bounds. Let's fix `blockDim.x = 128`. Grid: - gridDim.x = (W_out + 127) / 128 - gridDim.y = H_out - gridDim.z = N * C_out This gives good occupancy. Kernel: ``` int w = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int nc = blockIdx.z; int n = nc / C_out; int oc = nc % C_out; if (w >= W_out) return; float acc = bias[oc]; int c_per_group = C_in / groups; int g = oc / (C_out / groups); for (int ic = 0; ic < c_per_group; ++ic) { int c = g * c_per_group + ic; for (int kh = 0; kh < k_size; ++kh) { int ih = h * stride + kh - pad; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < k_size; ++kw) { int iw = w * stride + kw - pad; if (iw < 0 || iw >= W) continue; float val = in[((n * C_in + c) * H + ih) * W + iw]; float wt = weight[((oc * C_in / groups + ic) * k_size + kh) * k_size + kw]; acc += val * wt; } } } if (relu6) acc = fminf(fmaxf(acc, 0.0f), 6.0f); out[((n * C_out + oc) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = acc; ``` Wait, weight shape for grouped conv in PyTorch is [C_out, C_in/groups, k, k]. So indexing weight: for output channel oc, input channel within group ic, kernel kh,kw: weight[oc * (C_in/groups) * k * k + ic * k * k + kh * k + kw]. Weight indexing independent of group index g. Yes. So weight offset = ((oc * c_per_group + ic) * k + kh) * k + kw. Input indexing: `((n * C_in + g*c_per_group + ic) * H + ih) * W + iw`. This generic kernel works for all layers. Now, the final adaptive avg pool: we can write a simple kernel that reduces H*W to 1. ``` __global__ void adaptive_avg_pool_kernel(const float* in, float* out, int N, int C, int H, int W) { int nc = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (nc >= N*C) return; float sum = 0; for (int h=0; h<H; ++h) for (int w=0; w<W; ++w) sum += in[((nc)*H + h)*W + w]; out[nc] = sum / (H*W); } ``` Or use shared memory reduction per block. Given H,W small (max 112), a simple per-thread sum is okay. N*C = 10*1280 = 12800, so 128 threads per block => 100 blocks. Finally, linear layer: we can easily call `at::addmm` inside C++. Or write a kernel. Let's call `at::addmm(linear_bias, x, linear_weight.t())`. But we need to be careful about shape: x is [N,1280], linear_weight is [num_classes,1280]. So `at::addmm(linear_bias, x, linear_weight.t())` where linear_bias is [num_classes]. Actually `addmm` computes `bias + x @ weight.t()`. x is [N, C], weight.t() is [C, num_classes], bias is [num_classes]. That yields [N, num_classes]. Using ATen inside the extension is fine. Now, we need to write the host code that loops over layers. Since we have many layers, we could pass the metadata as tensors or lists. Let's define the Python side to store metadata as plain Python lists of ints. In the C++ code, we can accept `std::vector<int64_t>` for each attribute. But passing 7 vectors of length ~30 is fine. However, we need to be careful: `load_inline` will generate bindings for a C++ function with ATen and standard types. `std::vector<int64_t>` and `std::vector<at::Tensor>` are supported. Let's design the C++ host function signature: ```cpp at::Tensor mobilenetv2_forward_cuda( at::Tensor input, std::vector<at::Tensor> weights, std::vector<at::Tensor> biases, std::vector<int64_t> strides, std::vector<int64_t> paddings, std::vector<int64_t> groups, std::vector<int64_t> kernel_sizes, std::vector<int64_t> relu6_flags, at::Tensor linear_weight, at::Tensor linear_bias); ``` In the host function: - `input` is NCHW float32, shape [N,3,224,224] (or any N). - `x = input`. - For i in range(weights.size()): N = x.size(0) C_in = x.size(1) H = x.size(2) W = x.size(3) C_out = weights[i].size(0) k = kernel_sizes[i] stride = strides[i] pad = paddings[i] group = groups[i] H_out = (H + 2*pad - k) / stride + 1 W_out = (W + 2*pad - k) / stride + 1 relu6 = relu6_flags[i] allocate output tensor `y = at::empty({N, C_out, H_out, W_out}, x.options())` launch generic conv kernel. x = y. - After the conv layers, apply adaptive avg pool. - Then linear. We also need to handle the `features` list order. Let's build the metadata exactly. Since we ignore residual adds, the loop is straightforward. Let's list all the layers with their parameters. I will generate it systematically. Sequence of Convolution + BN (+ ReLU6) layers: 1. `Conv2d(3, 32, 3, stride=2, padding=1)` -> BN -> ReLU6. (kernel=3, stride=2, pad=1, groups=1, relu6=1) 2. Block [1,16,1,1]: - Dw Conv2d(32,32,3,s=1,p=1,g=32) -> BN -> ReLU6. (k=3,s=1,p=1,g=32,relu6=1) - Pw Conv2d(32,16,1) -> BN. (k=1,s=1,p=0,g=1,relu6=0) 3. Block [6,24,2,2]: Block1: - Pw Conv2d(16,96,1) -> BN -> ReLU6. (k=1,s=1,p=0,g=1,relu6=1) - Dw Conv2d(96,96,3,s=2,p=1,g=96) -> BN -> ReLU6. (k=3,s=2,p=1,g=96,relu6=1) - Pw Conv2d(96,24,1) -> BN. (k=1,s=1,p=0,g=1,relu6=0) Block2: - Pw Conv2d(24,144,1) -> BN -> ReLU6. (k=1,s=1,p=0,g=1,relu6=1) - Dw Conv2d(144,144,3,s=1,p=1,g=144) -> BN -> ReLU6. (k=3,s=1,p=1,g=144,relu6=1) - Pw Conv2d(144,24,1) -> BN. (k=1,s=1,p=0,g=1,relu6=0) 4. Block [6,32,3,2]: Block1: - Pw Conv2d(24,144,1) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Dw Conv2d(144,144,3,s=2,p=1,g=144) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Pw Conv2d(144,32,1) -> BN. Block2: - Pw Conv2d(32,192,1) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Dw Conv2d(192,192,3,s=1,p=1,g=192) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Pw Conv2d(192,32,1) -> BN. Block3: - Pw Conv2d(32,192,1) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Dw Conv2d(192,192,3,s=1,p=1,g=192) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Pw Conv2d(192,32,1) -> BN. 5. Block [6,64,4,2]: Block1: - Pw Conv2d(32,192,1) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Dw Conv2d(192,192,3,s=2,p=1,g=192) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Pw Conv2d(192,64,1) -> BN. Block2: - Pw Conv2d(64,384,1) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Dw Conv2d(384,384,3,s=1,p=1,g=384) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Pw Conv2d(384,64,1) -> BN. Block3: - same Block4: - same 6. Block [6,96,3,1]: Block1: - Pw Conv2d(64,384,1) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Dw Conv2d(384,384,3,s=1,p=1,g=384) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Pw Conv2d(384,96,1) -> BN. Block2: - Pw Conv2d(96,576,1) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Dw Conv2d(576,576,3,s=1,p=1,g=576) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Pw Conv2d(576,96,1) -> BN. Block3: - same 7. Block [6,160,3,2]: Block1: - Pw Conv2d(96,576,1) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Dw Conv2d(576,576,3,s=2,p=1,g=576) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Pw Conv2d(576,160,1) -> BN. Block2: - Pw Conv2d(160,960,1) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Dw Conv2d(960,960,3,s=1,p=1,g=960) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Pw Conv2d(960,160,1) -> BN. Block3: - same 8. Block [6,320,1,1]: Block1: - Pw Conv2d(160,960,1) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Dw Conv2d(960,960,3,s=1,p=1,g=960) -> BN -> ReLU6. - Pw Conv2d(960,320,1) -> BN. 9. Final: - Conv2d(320,1280,1) -> BN -> ReLU6. (k=1,s=1,p=0,g=1,relu6=1) 10. AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1,1)). 11. Linear(1280, num_classes). Total weight layers: let's count: 1 (initial) + 2 (block1) + 6 (block24) + 9 (block32) + 12 (block64) + 9 (block96) + 9 (block160) + 3 (block320) + 1 (final) = 52 layers. So `weights` vector length = 52. We can generate these parameters automatically in Python by iterating over `ref.features` and fusing. Fusing algorithm in Python: ```python ref = Model(num_classes) weights = [] biases = [] strides = [] paddings = [] groups = [] kernel_sizes = [] relu6_flags = [] i = 0 modules = list(ref.features.children()) while i < len(modules): m = modules[i] if isinstance(m, nn.Conv2d): conv = m bn = modules[i+1] i += 2 use_relu6 = False if i < len(modules) and isinstance(modules[i], nn.ReLU6): use_relu6 = True i += 1 # Fuse w = conv.weight.data mean = bn.running_mean var = bn.running_var gamma = bn.weight beta = bn.bias eps = bn.eps std = torch.sqrt(var + eps) # w shape [Cout, Cin/groups, k, k] # scale shape [Cout, 1, 1, 1] scale = (gamma / std).view(-1, 1, 1, 1) w_fused = w * scale b_fused = beta - mean * gamma / std weights.append(nn.Parameter(w_fused.contiguous(), requires_grad=False)) biases.append(nn.Parameter(b_fused.contiguous(), requires_grad=False)) strides.append(conv.stride[0]) paddings.append(conv.padding[0]) groups.append(conv.groups) kernel_sizes.append(conv.kernel_size[0]) relu6_flags.append(1 if use_relu6 else 0) elif isinstance(m, nn.AdaptiveAvgPool2d): i += 1 # skip, handle later elif isinstance(m, nn.ReLU6) or isinstance(m, nn.BatchNorm2d): # Should not happen individually after fusion i += 1 else: i += 1 # Also handle classifier linear linear = ref.classifier[1] linear_weight = nn.Parameter(linear.weight.data.clone().contiguous(), requires_grad=False) linear_bias = nn.Parameter(linear.bias.data.clone().contiguous(), requires_grad=False) ``` Wait: `nn.Parameter` with `requires_grad=False` is basically a parameter but no grad. We can also use `nn.Parameter(..., requires_grad=False)` or just store as buffers with `self.register_buffer`. Buffers are not parameters but will be saved. If we don't need backprop, buffers are fine. Let's store them as `self.register_buffer(name, tensor)`. But naming each one uniquely is annoying. Better: store them in lists and register each with `self.register_buffer(f"w{i}", tensor)`. Or use `self.w = nn.ParameterList([...])`. ParameterList is convenient. However, `ParameterList` expects `nn.Parameter` objects. We can set `requires_grad=False` to avoid grad overhead. But for the C++ extension, we need to pass the actual `Tensor` objects, which we can get from the parameter list. Now, writing the C++ host code: it needs to loop 52 times. For each iteration, extract params, compute output shape, allocate output, launch kernel. Let's write the generic conv kernel in CUDA. We should be careful about integer division and using `int` vs `int64_t`. Since dimensions are small, `int` is fine. ``` #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <vector> __global__ void fused_conv2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out, int H_out, int W_out, int k, int stride, int pad, int groups, bool relu6) { int w = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int nc = blockIdx.z; int n = nc / C_out; int oc = nc % C_out; if (w >= W_out || h >= H_out || n >= N) return; float acc = bias[oc]; int c_per_group = C_in / groups; int g = oc / (C_out / groups); for (int ic = 0; ic < c_per_group; ++ic) { int c = g * c_per_group + ic; for (int kh = 0; kh < k; ++kh) { int ih = h * stride + kh - pad; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < k; ++kw) { int iw = w * stride + kw - pad; if (iw < 0 || iw >= W) continue; float val = in[((n * C_in + c) * H + ih) * W + iw]; float wt = weight[((oc * c_per_group + ic) * k + kh) * k + kw]; acc += val * wt; } } } if (relu6) { acc = (acc > 0.0f) ? acc : 0.0f; acc = (acc < 6.0f) ? acc : 6.0f; } out[((n * C_out + oc) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = acc; } ``` This is straightforward. For the adaptive avg pool, we can write a kernel or just use `at::mean` on dimensions 2,3 with keepdim=False, then flatten. `x.mean({2,3})` returns [N,C]. We can use ATen ops for the pool and linear to reduce code. Let's check `at::mean` behavior: it reduces over dims, keeps other dims. `x.mean({2,3})` gives [N,C]. We can then call `at::addmm` for linear. Using ATen for these last two steps is fine and avoids writing more CUDA. However, `at::mean` might not be as fast as our custom kernel, but it's called once on a [N,1280,7,7] tensor. The overhead is negligible. Let's use a custom kernel anyway for avg pool to avoid any extra dispatch overhead? Actually dispatch overhead is tiny relative to the whole network, but writing a simple kernel for pool is easy. But it's okay to use ATen. For simplicity and fewer bugs, perhaps use ATen for pool and linear. Wait: does `x.mean({2,3})` keep the shape as [N,C] or [N,C,1,1]? The original code does `nn.AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1,1))` then `view`. The output of pool is [N,C,1,1]. Then `view` makes it [N,C]. Numerical result is the same. So in our host code, after the conv layers, we can call our custom avg pool kernel that directly writes [N,C] (flattened). Or we can allocate [N,C_out,1,1] and fill it. Let's write a simple kernel for adaptive avg pool to 1x1 that writes a tensor of shape [N, C_out, 1, 1] (or we can flatten to [N*C_out] and then reshape to [N, C_out] later). Actually the linear layer input needs [N, C_out]. Let's write a kernel that writes to [N, C_out] directly. ``` __global__ void adaptive_avg_pool_kernel(const float* in, float* out, int N, int C, int H, int W) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C; if (idx >= total) return; int base = idx * H * W; float sum = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < H * W; ++i) { sum += in[base + i]; } out[idx] = sum / (H * W); } ``` However, `in[base + i]` assumes that for each (n,c), the H*W elements are contiguous. In NCHW, for fixed n and c, the elements from h=0..H-1, w=0..W-1 are contiguous! Yes, because W is innermost. So `base = ((n*C + c)*H + 0)*W + 0 = (n*C + c)*H*W`. And `in[base + i]` iterates over h,w contiguously. Good. But note: `base = idx * H * W` is correct? If idx = n*C + c, then base = (n*C + c) * H * W. Since H*W is the product, and the offset for element (n,c,0,0) is (n*C + c)*H*W. Yes. So we can use `base = idx * H * W`. Great. For very large H*W (max 12544), each thread does 12544 loads. With 10*1280=12800 threads, total loads = ~161M. That's fine. Let's benchmark: per thread summing 12544 floats takes some time, but bandwidth is used well. Shared memory reduction might be better but complicated. For large H*W, the loop may have branch divergence? No. We can unroll? Not necessary. Occupancy: total threads = N*C. For first pool? Actually we only pool once at the end, on 7x7. Summing 49 elements per thread is negligible. So the generic conv kernel + simple pool + ATen linear should be the plan. One more thing: padding. For the generic conv kernel, the input shape after first conv is [N,32,112,112]. For subsequent layers, padding is always 0 or 1. With k=3, pad=1, stride can be 1 or 2. For stride 2, output size = floor((H + 2*1 - 3)/2) + 1 = floor((H-1)/2)+1. For H=224: (224+2-3)/2+1 = 223/2+1 = 111+1=112. Correct. For H=112 -> (112-1)/2+1 = 55.5 floor +1 = 56? Actually (112+2-3)/2 + 1 = 111/2 +1 = 55 +1 =56 (using integer division floor). Correct. For H=56 -> (56+2-3)/2+1 = 55/2+1=27+1=28. Correct. For H=28 -> (28+2-3)/2+1=27/2+1=13+1=14. Correct. For H=14 -> (14+2-3)/2+1=13/2+1=6+1=7. For odd H=7 -> (7+2-3)/2+1=6/2+1=3+1=4? Wait in MobileNetV2, when stride=2 and H=7, output should be 4? Let's check: the last stride=2 is in block [6,160,3,2], where dw goes from H=14 to H=7. After that, subsequent layers are stride=1. The final dw stride=2 occurs at block [6,160,3,2] with H=14 (input), H_out = floor((14+2-3)/2)+1 = 13/2+1 = 6+1=7. Actually (14+2*1-3) = 13, 13/2 = 6 (integer division), +1 = 7. So H_out=7. Correct. So for H=14, stride=2, output is 7. For H=7, stride=1 -> (7+2-3)/1+1 = 7. So the formula using integer division `(H + 2*pad - k) / stride + 1` works correctly in C++ for positive integers. Good. Now, we need to consider numerical tolerance. Our fused conv kernel essentially does a direct convolution, which may produce results slightly different from cuDNN's algorithms. cuDNN might use Winograd or FFT for some layers, which have different rounding. The tolerance is 1e-4. We need to check if our direct conv matches within 1e-4. Usually, for fp32, Winograd and direct differ by up to ~1e-5 to 1e-6 for typical random weights. However, for large sums (e.g., C_in=960, H=7), the error could be a bit larger but still likely < 1e-5 relative. Actually, the worst-case absolute error for summing 960 terms each ~1.0 with rounding ~1e-7 is about 1e-4 (sqrt(N)*1e-7 ~ 3e-6). So well within 1e-4. But there is one issue: the first convolution uses C_in=3, C_out=32, k=3. This is tiny. Direct conv will be fine. Now, writing the C++ host code: ```cpp at::Tensor mobilenetv2_forward_cuda( at::Tensor input, std::vector<at::Tensor> weights, std::vector<at::Tensor> biases, std::vector<int64_t> strides, std::vector<int64_t> paddings, std::vector<int64_t> groups, std::vector<int64_t> kernel_sizes, std::vector<int64_t> relu6_flags, at::Tensor linear_weight, at::Tensor linear_bias) { auto x = input.contiguous(); int num_layers = weights.size(); for (int i = 0; i < num_layers; ++i) { int N = x.size(0); int C_in = x.size(1); int H = x.size(2); int W = x.size(3); int C_out = weights[i].size(0); int k = kernel_sizes[i]; int stride = strides[i]; int pad = paddings[i]; int group = groups[i]; int H_out = (H + 2*pad - k) / stride + 1; int W_out = (W + 2*pad - k) / stride + 1; bool relu6 = relu6_flags[i] != 0; auto y = at::empty({N, C_out, H_out, W_out}, x.options()); dim3 block(128); dim3 grid((W_out + block.x - 1) / block.x, H_out, N * C_out); fused_conv2d_kernel<<<grid, block, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), weights[i].data_ptr<float>(), biases[i].data_ptr<float>(), y.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_in, H, W, C_out, H_out, W_out, k, stride, pad, group, relu6); x = y; } // Adaptive avg pool to 1x1 int N = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int H = x.size(2); int W = x.size(3); auto pooled = at::empty({N, C}, x.options()); int total_threads = N * C; dim3 pool_block(128); dim3 pool_grid((total_threads + pool_block.x - 1) / pool_block.x); adaptive_avg_pool_kernel<<<pool_grid, pool_block, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), pooled.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W); // Linear auto output = at::addmm(linear_bias, pooled, linear_weight.t()); return output; } ``` Need to include `ATen/cuda/CUDAContext.h` for `at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()`. Wait, is `linear_bias` shape [num_classes]? `at::addmm` expects bias to be 1D and broadcastable. The docs: `input + mat1 @ mat2`. Here bias is [num_classes], pooled is [N, C], linear_weight.t() is [C, num_classes]. `addmm` will broadcast bias over N. This should work. But we need to pass `linear_bias` as a 1D tensor. Actually, `at::addmm` signature: `Tensor addmm(const Tensor &self, const Tensor &mat1, const Tensor &mat2, Scalar beta=1, Scalar alpha=1)` where `self` is bias. If `self` is 1D, it is broadcast. So fine. The kernel code needs to be compiled with `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. We'll provide `cpp_sources` containing the C++ host function and `cuda_sources` containing the CUDA kernel. The `load_inline` call: ```python from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline _custom = load_inline( name="mobilenetv2_cuda", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["mobilenetv2_forward_cuda"], extra_cuda_cflags=["--use_fast_math", "-O3"], verbose=False, ) ``` Note: we should not define `PYBIND11_MODULE`. The `load_inline` function generates it. Now, we need to be careful about `std::vector<at::Tensor>` in pybind. pybind11 supports `std::vector<at::Tensor>` via `py::bind_vector` maybe, but since pybind11 is already configured for ATen types in PyTorch's cpp_extension, passing a Python list of Tensors should work. The C++ function signature with `std::vector<at::Tensor>` is standard. Next: The Python `ModelNew.__init__` logic. Since we need to fuse weights, let's write it carefully. ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, num_classes=1000): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.num_classes = num_classes ref = Model(num_classes) ref.eval() weights = [] biases = [] strides = [] paddings = [] groups = [] kernel_sizes = [] relu6_flags = [] modules = list(ref.features.children()) i = 0 while i < len(modules): m = modules[i] if isinstance(m, nn.Conv2d): conv = m bn = modules[i+1] assert isinstance(bn, nn.BatchNorm2d) i += 2 use_relu6 = False if i < len(modules) and isinstance(modules[i], (nn.ReLU6, nn.ReLU)): use_relu6 = True i += 1 # Fuse w = conv.weight.data.clone() mean = bn.running_mean var = bn.running_var gamma = bn.weight beta = bn.bias eps = bn.eps std = torch.sqrt(var + eps) # scale shape [Cout, 1, 1, 1] scale = (gamma / std).view(-1, 1, 1, 1) w_fused = w * scale b_fused = beta - mean * gamma / std weights.append(nn.Parameter(w_fused.contiguous(), requires_grad=False)) biases.append(nn.Parameter(b_fused.contiguous(), requires_grad=False)) strides.append(conv.stride[0] if isinstance(conv.stride, (tuple, list)) else conv.stride) paddings.append(conv.padding[0] if isinstance(conv.padding, (tuple, list)) else conv.padding) groups.append(conv.groups) kernel_sizes.append(conv.kernel_size[0] if isinstance(conv.kernel_size, (tuple, list)) else conv.kernel_size) relu6_flags.append(1 if use_relu6 else 0) else: i += 1 self.weights = nn.ParameterList(weights) self.biases = nn.ParameterList(biases) self.strides = strides self.paddings = paddings self.groups = groups self.kernel_sizes = kernel_sizes self.relu6_flags = relu6_flags linear = ref.classifier[1] self.linear_weight = nn.Parameter(linear.weight.data.clone().contiguous(), requires_grad=False) self.linear_bias = nn.Parameter(linear.bias.data.clone().contiguous(), requires_grad=False) # Optional: delete ref to save memory, but keep? We can keep since it might not matter. self.ref = ref ``` Wait: after the conv layers in `features`, there is `nn.AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1,1))`. We skip it in the loop. The loop will stop at `modules[i]` being pool, which is not a conv, so we increment i by 1 and finish. That's fine. But what about `nn.ReLU6(inplace=True)`? In the reference, ReLU6 is defined as `nn.ReLU6(inplace=True)`. `isinstance(modules[i], nn.ReLU6)` will work. However, `nn.ReLU` is different. The reference only uses ReLU6. Also, after extracting weights, we might want to move ref to CPU or delete it. But since the weights are Parameters, their underlying storage is shared with ref's parameters if we used `.data`? Actually, `conv.weight.data.clone()` returns a new tensor. The `nn.Parameter(w_fused.contiguous())` shares storage with `w_fused`. Since `w_fused` is derived from `w` (which is `.data.clone()`), it's a new tensor. So the reference model `ref` can be kept or discarded. We will keep it as an attribute? One concern: the C++ extension compilation. We need to provide correct includes. `torch/extension.h` includes everything. We also need `ATen/cuda/CUDAContext.h`. Also, need to handle `blockIdx.z` potentially exceeding the 16-bit limit on some GPUs? `blockIdx.z` is up to 65535 on older GPUs? In CUDA, grid dimensions are limited: x and y up to 2^31-1, z up to 65535. For some layers, `N * C_out` might exceed 65535? Let's compute max: N is batch size, typically <= maybe 32? In test, N=10. But to be safe, N*C_out for initial layer: 10*32=320. For C_out=1280, N=10 => 12800. That's well under 65535. Even if N=256, 256*1280=327680, which exceeds 65535. But the test uses N=10. Or use 1D grid index. Simpler: we can combine n and oc into a single linear block index and compute in kernel: ``` int total_nc = N * C_out; int block_id = blockIdx.z * gridDim.y * gridDim.x + blockIdx.y * gridDim.x + blockIdx.x; ``` But then we lose the 3D grid. Actually we can set grid as 2D: (w_blocks, H_out) and inside each block have threads process multiple `nc`. Or set grid as 1D and compute w, h, nc from block_id. Let's do a simple 1D grid: ``` int w_tile = 32; // or blockDim.x int w_blocks = (W_out + w_tile - 1) / w_tile; int total_blocks = w_blocks * H_out * N * C_out; dim3 block(128); dim3 grid((total_blocks + block.x - 1) / block.x); -- this is not right; each block handles one w column and one spatial point and one channel. We can use 1D grid where each block corresponds to one element of (w_block, h, n, c). Specifically: int w_tile = blockDim.x; int w_blocks = (W_out + w_tile - 1) / w_tile; int total_spatial = w_blocks * H_out; int blocks_per_batch_channel = total_spatial; int total_blocks = blocks_per_batch_channel * N * C_out; dim3 block(128); dim3 grid(total_blocks); ``` In kernel: ``` int block_id = blockIdx.x; int w = (block_id % w_blocks) * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int tmp = block_id / w_blocks; int h = tmp % H_out; int nc = tmp / H_out; int n = nc / C_out; int oc = nc % C_out; ``` But gridDim.x must be <= 2^31-1. total_blocks for max sizes: e.g., N=10, C_out=32, H_out=112, W_out=112, block.x=128, w_blocks=1 (since 112<128). total_blocks = 1*112*10*32 = 35840. Fine. For N=10, C_out=1280, H=7,W=7, block.x=128, w_blocks=1. total_blocks=1*7*10*1280=89600. Fine. For N=64, C_out=1280, H=112,W=112: total_blocks=1*112*64*1280 = 9,175,040. Fine. So 1D grid is safe. Let's use 1D grid with blockDim.x = 128. Kernel code: ``` __global__ void fused_conv2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out, int H_out, int W_out, int k, int stride, int pad, int groups, int relu6) { int w_blocks = (W_out + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; int block_id = blockIdx.x; int w = (block_id % w_blocks) * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int tmp = block_id / w_blocks; int h = tmp % H_out; int nc = tmp / H_out; int n = nc / C_out; int oc = nc % C_out; if (w >= W_out || h >= H_out || n >= N) return; float acc = bias[oc]; int c_per_group = C_in / groups; int g = oc / (C_out / groups); for (int ic = 0; ic < c_per_group; ++ic) { int c = g * c_per_group + ic; int base_in = ((n * C_in + c) * H + h * stride - pad) * W; int base_wt = ((oc * c_per_group + ic) * k) * k; for (int kh = 0; kh < k; ++kh) { int ih = h * stride + kh - pad; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < k; ++kw) { int iw = w * stride + kw - pad; if (iw < 0 || iw >= W) continue; float val = in[base_in + ih * W + iw]; float wt = weight[base_wt + kh * k + kw]; acc += val * wt; } } } if (relu6) { acc = fmaxf(acc, 0.0f); acc = fminf(acc, 6.0f); } out[((n * C_out + oc) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = acc; } ``` Wait, precomputing `base_in` as `((n*C_in + c) * H)` is okay, but then `ih` varies with `kh`. So we can compute `in_idx = ((n*C_in + c)*H + ih)*W + iw`. That's fine. Let's check `w_blocks`: if we compute it in kernel, it must be consistent with host. Host uses `w_blocks = (W_out + block.x - 1) / block.x`. Then total blocks = w_blocks * H_out * N * C_out. Yes. Now performance: for pointwise conv (k=1), the inner loops over kh,kw are trivial. For k=3, ic_per_group is often 1 (depthwise) or small (first conv C_in=3). So the kernel is fine. One more optimization: For pointwise (k=1), we can skip the kh/kw loops entirely, but the compiler will optimize it. However, the weight indexing still uses `kh*k+kw`, which for k=1 is just `base_wt`. It might not matter. Let's keep one generic kernel for simplicity. Another thing: because we use a 1D grid with blockDim.x=128, the number of threads per block is 128. When W_out < 128, w_blocks = 1. Each block handles one (h, n, oc) and 128 threads for W_out, but only W_out threads are active. That's fine. But for W_out small (e.g., 7), 121 threads are idle (only 7 active). That's not great for occupancy. Let's check: for H=7,W=7, C_out=1280, N=10: total blocks = 1 * 7 * 12800 = 89600 blocks. Each block has only 7 active threads. That's 128 threads launched but 121 do nothing. This is wasteful. However, the workload per active thread is large (C_in up to 960). So it's okay. But occupancy: many blocks, each with 1 warp partially active. The GPU can schedule many blocks, but SM utilization per block is low. Still, since each active thread does a lot of work, the ALUs are busy. Wait, but only 7 threads per block execute. For Ampere, a warp has 32 threads. If block has 128 threads = 4 warps. Only warp 0 has 7 active threads (21 idle), warps 1-3 are fully idle and exit immediately? Actually, the kernel returns early for threads where w >= W_out. If W_out=7 and blockDim.x=128, threads 0..6 are active, threads 7..127 return. Warps with all threads returning will be discarded quickly. So effectively we have one warp per block with 7 active lanes. That's okay, but not ideal. We could decrease blockDim.x for small W_out, but we don't know at compile time. We could set blockDim.x = min(128, W_out) in host, but CUDA block size must be fixed. Actually we can choose blockDim.x = 32 for all, since W_out is at least 1. For W_out=112, w_blocks = 4. So blocks per row = 4. That's fine. For W_out=7, w_blocks = 1, each block has 32 threads, 7 active. Still one warp with partial lanes. That's unavoidable. Using block=32 instead of 128 doesn't change much for small W_out. For large W_out, block=32 means fewer threads per block, but grid has more blocks (e.g., 112/32=4 blocks per row). That is okay. Block size of 32 might have lower occupancy per SM. Let's use block size = 64 as a compromise. Or we could set block size based on W_out: if W_out >= 128, block=128, else block=32? We can launch with different block sizes per layer, but that requires templating or multiple signatures. Simpler: just use block=32. It reduces partially active warps (warp size is 32). For W_out=7, 7 active threads in warp, 25 idle. For W_out=112, w_blocks=4, each block handles 32 columns. Grid size = 4*H*N*C_out. Blocks are smaller but more of them. Occupancy might be okay. Let's use block=32. Actually, 32 threads per block = 1 warp. GPU can run up to 64 warps per SM. So we can have up to 64 blocks per SM concurrently. That might be enough. Let's try block=32. Wait, for large layers, e.g., first conv: C_out=32, H=112, W=112, N=10. Blocks = (112/32=4) * 112 * 10 * 32 = 143,360 blocks. That's plenty. Each block does 32*3*3*3 ≈ 864 FLOPs. Fine. Let's use `blockDim.x = 32`. Adaptive avg pool kernel: block=256. grid = (N*C + 255)/256. The C++ host code: ```cpp at::Tensor mobilenetv2_forward_cuda( at::Tensor input, std::vector<at::Tensor> weights, std::vector<at::Tensor> biases, std::vector<int64_t> strides, std::vector<int64_t> paddings, std::vector<int64_t> groups, std::vector<int64_t> kernel_sizes, std::vector<int64_t> relu6_flags, at::Tensor linear_weight, at::Tensor linear_bias) { auto x = input.contiguous(); int64_t num_layers = weights.size(); for (int64_t i = 0; i < num_layers; ++i) { int64_t N = x.size(0); int64_t C_in = x.size(1); int64_t H = x.size(2); int64_t W = x.size(3); int64_t C_out = weights[i].size(0); int64_t k = kernel_sizes[i]; int64_t stride = strides[i]; int64_t pad = paddings[i]; int64_t group = groups[i]; int64_t H_out = (H + 2 * pad - k) / stride + 1; int64_t W_out = (W + 2 * pad - k) / stride + 1; int relu6 = relu6_flags[i]; auto y = at::empty({N, C_out, H_out, W_out}, x.options()); int w_threads = 32; int w_blocks = (W_out + w_threads - 1) / w_threads; int total_blocks = w_blocks * H_out * N * C_out; dim3 block(w_threads); dim3 grid(total_blocks); fused_conv2d_kernel<<<grid, block, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), weights[i].data_ptr<float>(), biases[i].data_ptr<float>(), y.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_in, H, W, C_out, H_out, W_out, k, stride, pad, group, relu6); // Optional: error check? For production, maybe not needed. x = y; } int64_t N = x.size(0); int64_t C = x.size(1); int64_t H = x.size(2); int64_t W = x.size(3); auto pooled = at::empty({N, C}, x.options()); int total_threads = N * C; dim3 pblock(256); dim3 pgrid((total_threads + pblock.x - 1) / pblock.x); adaptive_avg_pool_kernel<<<pgrid, pblock, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), pooled.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W); auto output = at::addmm(linear_bias, pooled, linear_weight.t()); return output; } ``` We need to declare the kernels before the host function. The CUDA source will contain both kernels and the host function. The C++ source will be empty or just declarations? `load_inline` with `cuda_sources` merges them. We can put everything in `cuda_sources` and supply an empty `cpp_sources` or just put the host function in `cpp_sources` and kernel in `cuda_sources`. Actually, for `load_inline`, the `cpp_sources` can be empty? Not sure. Usually `cpp_sources` contains host code, and `cuda_sources` contains the CUDA kernel and host launcher. But we can put the host function in `cpp_sources` as well if it uses CUDA Runtime (e.g., `<<< >>>`). However, files compiled by `nvcc` (cuda_sources) can contain both host and device code. Files compiled by the C++ compiler (cpp_sources) cannot contain `<<< >>>`. So if we put the host launcher in `cpp_sources`, it's compiled by `g++` and cannot launch kernels. But it's easier to put everything in `cuda_sources` and leave `cpp_sources` empty or minimal. Let's check `load_inline` docs. It can accept `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources`. The CUDA sources are compiled with nvcc. The C++ sources are compiled with the C++ compiler. To call a kernel from a function in `cpp_sources`, you need to declare the kernel and call it via a wrapper declared in `cpp_sources` but defined in `cuda_sources`? To avoid this complexity, we can put all host code (including pybind-exposed function) in `cuda_sources`. `load_inline` allows `cuda_sources` to contain host code. The pybind bindings are generated automatically for functions listed in `functions=[...]`. So the function can be in `cuda_sources`. That should work. Let's test this approach: in the `cuda_sources` string, define the kernel and the `at::Tensor mobilenetv2_forward_cuda(...)` function. Make sure to include `torch/extension.h` and `ATen/cuda/CUDAContext.h`. One more detail: `at::addmm` in `cuda_sources` is fine because it's ATen code (C++). The nvcc compiler can compile it. Also, we need to handle `blockIdx.x` overflow? Not needed for test sizes. Let's also consider `half`/`float16`. The problem says "Produce numerically equivalent outputs at fp32 precision." The input is `torch.rand(...)`, which is fp32. Our custom kernel uses `float`. That satisfies. Now, correctness. Let's think about the first conv: N=10, C_in=3, H=224, W=224. stride=2, pad=1, k=3. Our kernel loops over ic in 0..2, kh in 0..2, kw in 0..2. For each w and h, compute the addresses. The input shape is [10,3,224,224]. Offset formula: `((n*C_in + c)*H + ih)*W + iw`. That matches NCHW. Weight shape [32,3,3,3]. Offset formula: `((oc*C_in/groups + ic)*k + kh)*k + kw`. Since groups=1, C_in/groups=3. So offset = `((oc*3 + ic)*3 + kh)*3 + kw`. That's correct. For depthwise conv: e.g., oc=32, groups=32. c_per_group = C_in/groups = 1. For oc=0, g = 0/(32/32) = 0. ic=0, c=0. weight offset = ((0*1 + 0)*3 + kh)*3 + kw = kh*3+kw. Weight shape is [32,1,3,3]. So for oc=0, the first 9 elements. For oc=1, weight offset = ((1*1+0)*3+kh)*3+kw = 9 + kh*3+kw. Correct. For pointwise conv: k=1. `kh` loop runs once. `kw` loop runs once. `ih = h*stride - pad`. Since stride=1 and pad=0, ih=h. `iw = w*stride - pad = w`. Because k=1, the loops do exactly one iteration. Weight offset = ((oc * c_per_group + ic)*1 + 0)*1 + 0 = oc*c_per_group + ic. Weight shape [C_out, C_in, 1, 1]. Flattened to [C_out, C_in]. Correct. One issue: for stride=2 and pad=1, `ih = h*2 + kh - 1`. For h=0: ih = -1 + kh. kh=0 => -1 (skip), kh=1 => 0, kh=2 => 1. That's correct. ReLU6: `fmaxf(fminf(acc,6.0f),0.0f)`. Using `fminf` and `fmaxf`. Now, performance of this kernel. Let's analyze the theoretical speed. For each thread, the inner loops are not unrolled. Compilers may unroll for constant k if k is a template parameter. But k is a runtime variable. Using a template for k (1 or 3) and maybe stride/pad could help, but adds complexity. However, since k is small and the compiler may unroll loops with known trip counts? No, trip count is variable `k`. But we can manually branch: if k==1, skip loops. Or we can write a specialized pointwise kernel. Let's consider writing two versions: one for pointwise (k=1) and one for k=3. Since almost all layers are either 1x1 or 3x3, this is easy. Actually, we can write a single kernel but add a branch: ``` if (k == 1) { // direct 1x1 } else { // k==3 loop } ``` Since k is uniform across the kernel, this branch divergence is per block, not per thread. Actually, k is the same for all threads in a kernel launch. The compiler may not know it's uniform, but the condition `k == 1` is the same for all threads, so no intra-warp divergence. The compiler may still generate both paths. But for performance, we can create two kernels and choose based on `kernel_sizes[i]` in host. That's easy. `fused_conv1x1_kernel` and `fused_conv3x3_kernel`. The 1x1 version doesn't need kh/kw loops. Similarly, for pointwise, we might want to coalesce weight reads better. Let's write a specialized pointwise kernel: ``` __global__ void fused_conv1x1_kernel(...) { // same indexing float acc = bias[oc]; int c = oc * c_per_group; // wait, groups for pointwise is 1 so c_per_group = C_in, and g=oc // Actually for pointwise, groups=1, C_in/groups = C_in. // weight offset = oc*C_in + ic. // input offset = ((n*C_in + ic)*H + h)*W + w // We can loop over ic. int base_in = ((n * C_in) * H + h) * W + w; int base_wt = oc * C_in; #pragma unroll 4 for (int ic = 0; ic < C_in; ++ic) { float val = in[base_in + ic * H * W]; float wt = weight[base_wt + ic]; acc += val * wt; } // relu6... out[...] = acc; } ``` Wait, is `in[base_in + ic * H * W]` coalesced? For a fixed n,h,w, adjacent ic access elements separated by H*W. So no. But we can instead process a tile of ic in shared memory. Or we can swap: each thread handles one (n,oc) and a range of w? But then adjacent threads access same w? Not good. Alternatively, note that in the generic kernel, adjacent threads read adjacent w for the same ic, which IS coalesced. The weight is broadcast. This is fine. We don't need a separate pointwise kernel for coalescing. The generic kernel already does this for k=1 (since iw=w). So the generic kernel is good for 1x1. Let's keep one generic kernel for k=1 and k=3 to reduce code size and debugging. It is simple and likely fast enough. The speedup will come from fusing with BN and ReLU6, as well as avoiding Python overhead and intermediate allocations. Let's think about memory allocation. In the host code, we allocate `y` for each layer. That's 52 allocations. PyTorch's eager mode also does allocations for each layer. Here we are doing similar. But we could use a double-buffering scheme with two pre-allocated buffers to avoid some allocations. However, the shapes change, so we would need the max size buffer. For simplicity, `at::empty` per layer is okay. But the allocation overhead in PyTorch's `at::empty` is small (it just reserves memory from the caching allocator). Since we have 52 layers, 52 allocations is fine. The reference does many more allocations because each Conv2d, BN, ReLU6 allocate output tensors. Fusing them into one kernel eliminates intermediate allocations. So that's a win. Actually, in the reference, a Conv2d layer calls cuDNN which allocates workspace internally, but the output tensor is the main allocation. BN allocates another. ReLU6 (inplace=True) might reuse the same buffer? Wait, `nn.ReLU6(inplace=True)` modifies the tensor in-place. So BN output is passed to ReLU6, which modifies it in place. So no allocation for ReLU6. But BN still allocates its output. And Conv2d allocates output. So per fused conv+bn+relu6, the reference does 2 allocations (conv out, bn out). Our custom does 1 (fused out). So we save ~50% of activation allocations. Also, memory traffic: reference reads input for conv, writes conv out, reads conv out for bn, writes bn out, reads bn out for relu6, writes bn out (inplace). So total memory: read input, write conv_out, read conv_out, write bn_out, read bn_out, write bn_out. That's 3 reads + 3 writes for each layer. Our fused kernel: read input, write fused_out. Plus weight/bias reads. So 1 read + 1 write. That's a 3x reduction in activation memory traffic. Since MobileNetV2 is memory-bound, this should be a significant speedup. So this approach should be faster than the reference, even with a naive conv implementation. Let's double-check the BN fusion math. `y = gamma * (conv(x) - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) + beta` `y = conv(x) * gamma / sqrt(var + eps) + (beta - mean * gamma / sqrt(var + eps))` Since we fuse into `w_fused = w * (gamma / sqrt(var+eps)).reshape(-1,1,1,1)`, and `b_fused = beta - mean * gamma / sqrt(var+eps)`. Yes. If `conv.bias` exists (it doesn't in the reference), we need to include it, but here it's None. If `bn.bias` is None? In reference, BatchNorm2d has affine=True by default, so weight (gamma) and bias (beta) exist. Let's test with a simple small conv+bn+relu6 in Python to ensure fusion math matches kernel output. It should, to within floating point error. Now, let's think about the adaptive avg pool. The original reference uses `nn.AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1,1))`. For a tensor of shape [N,C,H,W], it computes the mean over H and W, yielding [N,C,1,1]. Our kernel computes `sum / (H*W)`. That's mathematically equivalent. But we need to be careful about integer division: `H*W` is int, but we need float division: `sum / float(H*W)`. For the linear layer, reference uses `nn.Linear(1280, num_classes)`. This computes `x @ W.T + b`. Our `at::addmm(linear_bias, pooled, linear_weight.t())` does the same. Wait: `at::addmm` computes `beta * input + alpha * (mat1 @ mat2)`. With default beta=1, alpha=1. So `bias + pooled @ linear_weight.t()`. Yes. Now, we should test the compilation and correctness. But since we have many layers, manual hardcoding of metadata in C++ is not needed because we pass vectors. The Python side extracts them automatically by iterating `ref.features`. The C++ side loops over the vectors. This is robust. Let's consider the possibility that the evaluation environment might compile the extension each time the module is imported, which is slow. But `load_inline` caches compiled extensions based on source code hash and build directory. It should be fine. Actually, we need to make sure the extension name is unique and that we don't get collisions. Use a descriptive name like `mobilenetv2_cuda_ext`. Let's assume `Model` is available. Let's draft the full code. Note: `load_inline` can be called at module level. The resulting module is stored in a global variable `_mobilenetv2_cuda`. Then in `ModelNew.forward`, we call `_mobilenetv2_cuda.mobilenetv2_forward_cuda(...)`. Let's write the kernel source carefully. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cfloat> __global__ void fused_conv2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out, int H_out, int W_out, int k, int stride, int pad, int groups, int relu6) { int w_threads = blockDim.x; int w_blocks = (W_out + w_threads - 1) / w_threads; int block_id = blockIdx.x; int w = (block_id % w_blocks) * w_threads + threadIdx.x; int tmp = block_id / w_blocks; int h = tmp % H_out; int nc = tmp / H_out; int n = nc / C_out; int oc = nc % C_out; if (w >= W_out || h >= H_out || n >= N) return; float acc = bias[oc]; int c_per_group = C_in / groups; int g = oc / (C_out / groups); for (int ic = 0; ic < c_per_group; ++ic) { int c = g * c_per_group + ic; for (int kh = 0; kh < k; ++kh) { int ih = h * stride + kh - pad; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < k; ++kw) { int iw = w * stride + kw - pad; if (iw < 0 || iw >= W) continue; float val = in[((n * C_in + c) * H + ih) * W + iw]; float wt = weight[((oc * c_per_group + ic) * k + kh) * k + kw]; acc += val * wt; } } } if (relu6) { acc = fmaxf(acc, 0.0f); acc = fminf(acc, 6.0f); } out[((n * C_out + oc) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = acc; } __global__ void adaptive_avg_pool_kernel( const float* __restrict__ in, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C, int H, int W) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C; if (idx >= total) return; float sum = 0.0f; int base = idx * H * W; int hw = H * W; for (int i = 0; i < hw; ++i) { sum += in[base + i]; } out[idx] = sum / float(hw); } at::Tensor mobilenetv2_forward_cuda( at::Tensor input, std::vector<at::Tensor> weights, std::vector<at::Tensor> biases, std::vector<int64_t> strides, std::vector<int64_t> paddings, std::vector<int64_t> groups, std::vector<int64_t> kernel_sizes, std::vector<int64_t> relu6_flags, at::Tensor linear_weight, at::Tensor linear_bias) { auto x = input.contiguous(); int64_t num_layers = weights.size(); for (int64_t i = 0; i < num_layers; ++i) { int64_t N = x.size(0); int64_t C_in = x.size(1); int64_t H = x.size(2); int64_t W = x.size(3); int64_t C_out = weights[i].size(0); int64_t k = kernel_sizes[i]; int64_t stride = strides[i]; int64_t pad = paddings[i]; int64_t group = groups[i]; int64_t H_out = (H + 2 * pad - k) / stride + 1; int64_t W_out = (W + 2 * pad - k) / stride + 1; int relu6 = relu6_flags[i] ? 1 : 0; auto y = at::empty({N, C_out, H_out, W_out}, x.options()); int w_threads = 32; int w_blocks = (W_out + w_threads - 1) / w_threads; int total_blocks = w_blocks * H_out * N * C_out; dim3 block(w_threads); dim3 grid(total_blocks); fused_conv2d_kernel<<<grid, block, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), weights[i].data_ptr<float>(), biases[i].data_ptr<float>(), y.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_in, H, W, C_out, H_out, W_out, k, stride, pad, group, relu6); x = y; } int64_t N = x.size(0); int64_t C = x.size(1); int64_t H = x.size(2); int64_t W = x.size(3); auto pooled = at::empty({N, C}, x.options()); int total_threads = N * C; dim3 pblock(256); dim3 pgrid((total_threads + pblock.x - 1) / pblock.x); adaptive_avg_pool_kernel<<<pgrid, pblock, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), pooled.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W); auto output = at::addmm(linear_bias, pooled, linear_weight.t()); return output; } """ cpp_source = "" # empty _mobilenetv2_cuda = load_inline( name="mobilenetv2_cuda_ext", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["mobilenetv2_forward_cuda"], extra_cuda_cflags=["--use_fast_math", "-O3"], verbose=False, ) ``` Wait, `load_inline` needs the function to be declared in the C++ sources if `cpp_sources` is used? Actually, pybind will scan all source files. I think putting everything in `cuda_sources` and leaving `cpp_sources` empty should work. Let's verify: `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` compiles the given sources into a shared library. `cuda_sources` is compiled by nvcc into a .cu.o object, `cpp_sources` by C++ compiler. Then linked together. If `cpp_sources` is empty, only the CUDA object is linked, which is fine. The pybind11 auto-generated module will look for the functions in the linked objects. Since `mobilenetv2_forward_cuda` is defined in the CUDA object, it should be found. So okay. One more thing: the function needs to be declared with `extern "C"`? No, C++ name mangling is fine; pybind11 uses the function signature. However, `load_inline` might need at least one `cpp_sources`? I think it's okay to pass empty string. Let's be safe and put the host function in both? No, `cpp_sources` cannot contain CUDA syntax. So leave it empty. Let's think about potential compilation issues. `torch/extension.h` includes `ATen/ATen.h`, etc. It should compile. `at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()` may require `#include <ATen/cuda/CUDAContext.h>`. Let's include that. Another issue: `std::vector<at::Tensor>` bindings. According to PyTorch docs, `load_inline` uses `pybind11/stl.h` which supports `std::vector`. And ATen tensors are supported. So passing a list of Python `torch.Tensor` objects should bind to `std::vector<at::Tensor>`. Now, let's write the Python `ModelNew`. ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, num_classes=1000): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.num_classes = num_classes ref = Model(num_classes) ref.eval() weights = [] biases = [] strides = [] paddings = [] groups = [] kernel_sizes = [] relu6_flags = [] modules = list(ref.features.children()) idx = 0 while idx < len(modules): m = modules[idx] if isinstance(m, nn.Conv2d): conv = m bn = modules[idx + 1] assert isinstance(bn, nn.BatchNorm2d) idx += 2 use_relu6 = False if idx < len(modules) and isinstance(modules[idx], nn.ReLU6): use_relu6 = True idx += 1 # Fuse BN into conv w = conv.weight.data.clone() mean = bn.running_mean var = bn.running_var gamma = bn.weight beta = bn.bias eps = bn.eps std = torch.sqrt(var + eps) scale = (gamma / std).view(-1, 1, 1, 1) w_fused = w * scale b_fused = beta - mean * gamma / std weights.append(nn.Parameter(w_fused.contiguous(), requires_grad=False)) biases.append(nn.Parameter(b_fused.contiguous(), requires_grad=False)) s = conv.stride p = conv.padding k = conv.kernel_size strides.append(s[0] if isinstance(s, (tuple, list)) else s) paddings.append(p[0] if isinstance(p, (tuple, list)) else p) groups.append(conv.groups) kernel_sizes.append(k[0] if isinstance(k, (tuple, list)) else k) relu6_flags.append(1 if use_relu6 else 0) else: idx += 1 self.weights = nn.ParameterList(weights) self.biases = nn.ParameterList(biases) self.strides = strides self.paddings = paddings self.groups = groups self.kernel_sizes = kernel_sizes self.relu6_flags = relu6_flags linear = ref.classifier[1] self.linear_weight = nn.Parameter(linear.weight.data.clone().contiguous(), requires_grad=False) self.linear_bias = nn.Parameter(linear.bias.data.clone().contiguous(), requires_grad=False) def forward(self, x): w_list = list(self.weights) b_list = list(self.biases) return _mobilenetv2_cuda.mobilenetv2_forward_cuda( x, w_list, b_list, self.strides, self.paddings, self.groups, self.kernel_sizes, self.relu6_flags, self.linear_weight, self.linear_bias, ) ``` Wait: `self.weights` is a `nn.ParameterList`. Iterating over it yields Parameters (which are Tensors). Passing a list of Tensors to the C++ function should work. However, pybind might need exactly `std::vector<at::Tensor>`. A `torch.nn.Parameter` inherits from `Tensor`, so it should be recognized. Let's test mentally: `w_list` is a Python list of Parameters. When calling the bound function, pybind will convert each element to `at::Tensor`. That should work. Let's consider the `Model` reference. Is it guaranteed to be defined? Yes. But to be safe, we can import it? It's in the same global namespace. When `exec()` is called with our code, the globals mapping should include everything previously defined. So we can reference `Model`. Is there any chance that `ref.features` contains `nn.Sequential` wrappers? No, the code appended each `nn.Sequential` block to `features`. `list(ref.features.children())` returns the modules inside `features`. That includes Conv2d, BatchNorm2d, ReLU6, and also the `nn.Sequential` objects returned by `_inverted_residual_block`. Inside those `nn.Sequential`s are Conv2d, BN, ReLU6. Wait! In the reference, `features.append(_inverted_residual_block(...)[0])` appends a `nn.Sequential`. So `ref.features.children()` will be a list like: ``` [Conv2d(3,32,...), BatchNorm2d(32), ReLU6(), Sequential(Conv2d(32,32,...), BatchNorm2d(32), ReLU6(), Conv2d(32,16,...), BatchNorm2d(16)), Sequential(...), ...] ``` So iterating `list(ref.features.children())` will yield the `nn.Sequential` blocks as entire modules. It will NOT yield the individual Conv2d layers inside those blocks. Our current fusion logic assumes that every Conv2d is a direct child of `features`, which is wrong. We need to recursively flatten `features` to extract all Conv2d+BN+ReLU6 sequences. We can write a helper function `extract_layers(module_list)` that recursively iterates over all modules in order. For each module in order: - If it's a Conv2d, the next should be BN, then optionally ReLU6. - If it's a Sequential, recurse into its children (in order). - If it's AdaptiveAvgPool2d, skip or mark. But note, inside the Sequential blocks, the layers are exactly: [Conv2d, BN, ReLU6, Conv2d, BN, ...] etc. There might be a ReLU6 after BN, but not after the last BN in the block (pointwise linear). So the pattern Conv2d -> BN -> maybe ReLU6 holds inside the sequential as well. So we can write a recursive generator: ```python def iter_modules(seq): for m in seq.children(): if isinstance(m, nn.Sequential): yield from iter_modules(m) else: yield m ``` Then `modules = list(iter_modules(ref.features))`. This yields a flat list of all primitive layers in order. Let's list them to check: - Conv2d(3,32) - BN(32) - ReLU6 - Conv2d(32,32) [first block dw] - BN(32) - ReLU6 - Conv2d(32,16) [first block pw] - BN(16) - Conv2d(16,96) [expand] - BN(96) - ReLU6 - Conv2d(96,96) [dw] - BN(96) - ReLU6 - Conv2d(96,24) [pw] - BN(24) ... and so on. It's a flat list of length ~140 (conv+bn+relu6 primitive modules). Then our fusion loop can process it sequentially. Let's adjust the Python code. ```python def _flatten(module): for child in module.children(): if isinstance(child, nn.Sequential): yield from _flatten(child) else: yield child modules = list(_flatten(ref.features)) ``` Then the while loop as before. Also, after extracting, we don't need to keep `ref` alive? We need to keep the weights in `self.weights`, etc., which are copies. Let's think about memory: we clone weight and bias data, so total parameter count is roughly same as reference. That should be fine. One potential issue: After fusion, `w_fused` and `b_fused` require gradient? We set `requires_grad=False`. Good. If we want to be able to train ModelNew, we'd need more code, but the task only asks for forward pass. Another issue: The `ref` model is created in `__init__`. If we do this on CPU, it's fast. But if we do it on GPU, it might allocate memory. `Model(num_classes)` defaults to CPU. We don't need to move it to GPU. The forward call moves input to the custom extension, which runs on GPU. The weights in our Parameters are on CPU until forward is called? Actually, PyTorch modules register parameters on CPU by default. If the input `x` is on GPU, the custom extension will need the weights on GPU. But we don't call `.to(x.device)` on them in forward. So if the model is on CPU and input is on GPU, the extension will try to launch CUDA kernels with CPU tensors, causing an error. Usually, in PyTorch, the model should be on the same device as input.
