If we register Parameters, calling `model.to('cuda')` will move them to GPU. The `register_parameter` and `register_buffer` cause them to be moved. Good. So we should register weights and biases as parameters or buffers. `nn.ParameterList` works, but we need to also make sure the linear weight and bias are registered as `nn.Parameter`. Yes. Another thing: the metadata lists (`strides`, etc.) are just Python lists of ints. They stay on CPU. The extension receives them as `std::vector<int64_t>`. Good. Now, what about `AdaptiveAvgPool2d`? In the flat list, it will appear after the last ReLU6. Our while loop will encounter it, see it's not Conv2d, increment idx by 1, and finish. That's fine. Let's also consider `nn.Dropout` in classifier. Since we only use the linear layer, we ignore dropout. Let's check correctness of output shapes. Input: [N,3,224,224]. Layer 0: k=3, s=2, p=1 -> H=(224+2-3)/2+1=112. W=112. Out: [N,32,112,112]. Block1: no expand. dw k=3,s=1,p=1 -> 112. pw k=1,s=1,p=0 -> 112. Out: [N,16,112,112]. Block24: pw 16->96 -> 112. dw s=2 -> (112+2-3)/2+1 = 56. pw -> 56. Out: [N,24,56,56]. Then next pw from 24->144, dw s=1 -> 56, pw -> 56. Out: [N,24,56,56]. ... and so on. All output sizes should match. Let's test mentally with kernel indexing for pointwise: stride=1, pad=0, k=1. `ih = h*1 + 0 - 0 = h`. `iw = w*1 + 0 - 0 = w`. Good. For dw with stride=2, pad=1: `ih = h*2 + kh - 1`. For h=0: ih = kh-1. Good. For h=H_out-1 = floor((H+1)/2)-1? Let's not worry; formula is standard. Now, performance considerations: our generic kernel uses loops over ic, kh, kw. For depthwise conv, c_per_group=1, so ic loop runs once. For pointwise, k=1, so kh and kw loops run once. The compiler will likely inline and unroll the inner loops if they are small constants? No, k is a runtime parameter. However, we can pass k as template parameter by instantiating two kernels and calling the correct one. This might improve performance because the loops can be unrolled for k=1 and k=3. Since we only have 1x1 and 3x3, we can template on K. And similarly for relu6. But we can just pass them as arguments and hope the compiler optimizes. For k=1, the inner loops have trip count 1 but the compiler doesn't know. Actually, the compiler only knows it's a variable loaded from constant memory? No, it's passed as a kernel argument (in a register), so the compiler sees it as a variable. It might not unroll. To squeeze more performance, we can create separate kernels for k=1 and k=3, and another for relu6/no relu6. Let's define: ``` template <bool RELU6, int K> __global__ void fused_conv2d_kernel_t(...) ``` But `K` as a template parameter means we need to instantiate for each K we use (1 and 3). That's easy. In the host code, we can dispatch based on `k`. ``` if (k == 1) { fused_conv2d_kernel_t<true, 1><<<...>>>(..., relu6); fused_conv2d_kernel_t<false, 1><<<...>>>(..., relu6); } else if (k == 3) { // launch k=3 version } ``` Wait, but relu6 is also a runtime param. We could use template for relu6 too: `template <bool RELU6, int K>`. Then dispatch with switch: ``` if (k == 1) { if (relu6) fused_conv2d_kernel_t<true,1><<<...>>>(...); else fused_conv2d_kernel_t<false,1><<<...>>>(...); } else if (k == 3) { if (relu6) fused_conv2d_kernel_t<true,3><<<...>>>(...); else fused_conv2d_kernel_t<false,3><<<...>>>(...); } ``` This avoids dynamic k inside the kernel, allowing loop unrolling and better register usage. The code for the kernel with runtime k is fairly simple, but using templates is a small change and could help. Let's do it. The kernel body becomes: ```cpp template <bool RELU6, int K> __global__ void fused_conv2d_kernel_t(...) { //... for (int ic = 0; ic < c_per_group; ++ic) { int c = g * c_per_group + ic; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int ih = h * stride + kh - pad; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H) continue; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < K; ++kw) { int iw = w * stride + kw - pad; if (iw < 0 || iw >= W) continue; float val = in[((n * C_in + c) * H + ih) * W + iw]; float wt = weight[((oc * c_per_group + ic) * K + kh) * K + kw]; acc += val * wt; } } } if (RELU6) { ... } // write } ``` Then we only instantiate for K=1 and K=3. Since we only have these two kernel sizes, this is fine. For K=1, the compiler will eliminate the kh and kw loops, simplifying indexing. For K=3, it will unroll the loops. Let's update the host accordingly. One more nuance: `pad` can be 0 or 1. For K=1, pad is 0. The compiler doesn't know `pad` at compile time, but the branch `if (ih < 0 ...)` will be `if (h*stride < 0 || h*stride >= H)` which is always false for valid h. Actually with K=1 and pad=0, `ih = h*stride`. Since h ranges 0..H_out-1 and stride=1, ih=h which is always valid. `iw = w*stride` always valid. So the boundary checks could be removed by optimizer? Probably not because `pad` is a variable. But it's okay. At this point, we should write the final code. But we need to test compilation and correctness. The environment has the tools. Let's first write the kernel code, then compile it (using `compile_kernel` tool), then check correctness. 1) / w_threads;\n int total_blocks = w_blocks * H_out * N * C_out;\n dim3 block(w_threads);\n dim3 grid(total_blocks);\n if (k == 1) {\n if (use_relu6) {\n fused_conv2d_kernel_t<true, 1><<<grid, block, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>(\n x.data_ptr<float>(), weights[i].data_ptr<float>(), biases[i].data_ptr<float>(), y.data_ptr<float>(),\n N, C_in, H, W, C_out, H_out, W_out, stride, pad, group);\n } else {\n fused_conv2d_kernel_t<false, 1><<<grid, block, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>(\n x.data_ptr<float>(), weights[i].data_ptr<float>(), biases[i].data_ptr<float>(), y.data_ptr<float>(),\n N, C_in, H, W, C_out, H_out, W_out, stride, pad, group);\n }\n } else {\n if (use_relu6) {\n fused_conv2d_kernel_t<true, 3><<<grid, block, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>(\n x.data_ptr<float>(), weights[i].data_ptr<float>(), biases[i].data_ptr<float>(), y.data_ptr<float>(),\n N, C_in, H, W, C_out, H_out, W_out, stride, pad, group);\n } else {\n fused_conv2d_kernel_t<false, 3><<<grid, block, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>(\n x.data_ptr<float>(), weights[i].data_ptr<float>(), biases[i].data_ptr<float>(), y.data_ptr<float>(),\n N, C_in, H, W, C_out, H_out, W_out, stride, pad, group);\n }\n }\n x = y;\n }\n int64_t N = x.size(0);\n int64_t C = x.size(1);\n int64_t H = x.size(2);\n int64_t W = x.size(3);\n auto pooled = at::empty({N, C}, x.options());\n int total_threads = N * C;\n dim3 pblock(256);\n dim3 pgrid((total_threads + pblock.x - 1) / pblock.x);\n adaptive_avg_pool_kernel<<<pgrid, pblock, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>(\n x.data_ptr<float>(), pooled.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W);\n auto output = at::addmm(linear_bias, pooled, linear_weight.t());\n return output;\n}\n\"\"\"\n\n_mobilenetv2_cuda = load_inline(\n name=\"mobilenetv2_cuda_ext\",\n cpp_sources=\"\",\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=[\"mobilenetv2_forward_cuda\"],\n extra_cuda_cflags=[\"--use_fast_math\", \"-O3\"],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, num_classes=1000):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.num_classes = num_classes\n ref = Model(num_classes)\n ref.eval()\n\n weights = []\n biases = []\n strides = []\n paddings = []\n groups = []\n kernel_sizes = []\n relu6_flags = []\n\n def iter_modules(module):\n for child in module.children():\n if isinstance(child, nn.Sequential):\n yield from iter_modules(child)\n else:\n yield child\n\n modules = list(iter_modules(ref.features))\n idx = 0\n while idx < len(modules):\n m = modules[idx]\n if isinstance(m, nn.Conv2d):\n conv = m\n bn = modules[idx + 1]\n assert isinstance(bn, nn.BatchNorm2d)\n idx += 2\n use_relu6 = False\n if idx < len(modules) and isinstance(modules[idx], nn.ReLU6):\n use_relu6 = True\n idx += 1\n # Fuse BN into conv\n w = conv.weight.data.clone()\n mean = bn.running_mean\n var = bn.running_var\n gamma = bn.weight\n beta = bn.bias\n eps = bn.eps\n std = torch.sqrt(var + eps)\n scale = (gamma / std).view(-1, 1, 1, 1)\n w_fused = w * scale\n b_fused = beta - mean * gamma / std\n weights.append(nn.Parameter(w_fused.contiguous(), requires_grad=False))\n biases.append(nn.Parameter(b_fused.contiguous(), requires_grad=False))\n s = conv.stride\n p = conv.padding\n k = conv.kernel_size\n strides.append(s[0] if isinstance(s, (tuple, list)) else s)\n paddings.append(p[0] if isinstance(p, (tuple, list)) else p)\n groups.append(conv.groups)\n kernel_sizes.append(k[0] if isinstance(k, (tuple, list)) else k)\n relu6_flags.append(1 if use_relu6 else 0)\n else:\n idx += 1\n\n self.weights = nn.ParameterList(weights)\n self.biases = nn.ParameterList(biases)\n self.strides = strides\n self.paddings = paddings\n self.groups = groups\n self.kernel_sizes = kernel_sizes\n self.relu6_flags = relu6_flags\n\n linear = ref.classifier[1]\n self.linear_weight = nn.Parameter(linear.weight.data.clone().contiguous(), requires_grad=False)\n self.linear_bias = nn.Parameter(linear.bias.data.clone().contiguous(), requires_grad=False)\n\n def forward(self, x):\n w_list = list(self.weights)\n b_list = list(self.biases)\n return _mobilenetv2_cuda.mobilenetv2_forward_cuda(\n x,\n w_list,\n b_list,\n self.strides,\n self.paddings,\n self.groups,\n self.kernel_sizes,\n self.relu6_flags,\n self.linear_weight,\n self.linear_bias,\n )\n" }

Here's a plan: In C++: ```cpp // Forward function that calls at::conv2d in a loop with fused bn+relu parameters at::Tensor mobilenetv2_forward_cuda( at::Tensor input, std::vector<at::Tensor> weights, std::vector<at::Tensor> biases, std::vector<int64_t> strides, std::vector<int64_t> paddings, std::vector<int64_t> groups, std::vector<int64_t> kernel_sizes, std::vector<int64_t> relu6_flags, at::Tensor linear_weight, at::Tensor linear_bias) { auto x = input; for (size_t i = 0; i < weights.size(); ++i) { x = at::conv2d(x, weights[i], biases[i], strides[i], paddings[i], 1, groups[i]); if (relu6_flags[i]) { x = at::clamp_min(x, 0).clamp_max_(6); // or x = at::relu6(x); } } x = at::adaptive_avg_pool2d(x, {1, 1}); x = x.view({x.size(0), -1}); x = at::addmm(linear_bias, x, linear_weight.t()); return x; } ``` But `at::conv2d` in C++ requires `torch/libtorch` headers. For batch size 10, we might have enough parallelism. Actually, input layout is NCHW. For 1x1 conv, each output pixel (n, oc, h, w) is a dot product over c. Coalesce by having threads in a warp access different c but same n,h,w, then each thread in a warp accesses input[n, thread_lane, h, w]. This is bad coalescing because c is the channel dimension, which in NCHW means stride of H*W. Actually, if thread 0 reads c=0, thread 1 reads c=1, etc., the addresses are separated by H*W floats. Actually, the most standard approach: each block handles a tile of output channels and spatial positions. Actually, each thread reads 9 floats, but adjacent threads share many of them spatially. The GPU L1 will cache these. Effective bandwidth per element is about 1x for interior values. So the memory bandwidth is about N*C*H*W * 4 bytes for input + N*C*H*W * 4 bytes for output = roughly 2*4M*4 = 32 MB. At 1 TB/s, that's 0.032 ms. Plus some overhead. For a block of OC threads and IW (input width) elements: Actually, standard "direct" 1x1 conv kernel for NCHW: - Each thread computes one output (n, oc, h, w). - Loop over ic from 0 to C_in-1. - Memory access: input[n, ic, h, w] and weight[oc, ic]. - To optimize, preload weight into shared memory or rely on L1 cache since each warp reads the same set of weights (broadcast). Actually, if a warp has threads with different `oc`, they read different weights. So weight access is not uniform. But if blockDim.x covers `w` and blockDim.y covers `oc`, then threads in the same w but different oc read different weights. Threads in different w but same oc read same weights. Actually, best layout: warp handles same oc, but different w. Then weight[] is uniform, and input[] is coalesced. - Block: (w_tile, oc_tile, 1) - Grid: (w_tiles, h, n * oc_tiles) Let's say w_tile = 32, oc_tile = 8. Block size = 256. Each thread in the block: - n = blockIdx.z / oc_tiles - oc_block = blockIdx.z % oc_tiles - h = blockIdx.y - w = blockIdx.x * w_tile + threadIdx.x - oc = oc_block * oc_tile + threadIdx.y Then each thread loops over ic and accumulates input[n, ic, h, w] * weight[oc, ic, ...]. This is actually quite good: threads in a warp (threadIdx.x varies) access consecutive w -> coalesced. threadIdx.y is uniform per warp if warp size divides w_tile, or if warp consists of 32 threads with varying x and y, we need to be careful. Actually, in CUDA a warp is usually 32 threads with linear thread ID 0..31. If blockDim.x=32, blockDim.y=8, then thread IDs: - tid 0..31: threadIdx.y = 0 (since 32/32=0), threadIdx.x = 0..31. So a warp is 32 threads all with oc_block*oc_tile + 0. They all have the same oc. They access different w. Weight is uniform. Input is coalesced across w. This is perfect! - tid 32..63: threadIdx.y=1, threadIdx.x=0..31. Same oc, coalesced w. Also perfect. So block=(32, 8), threads per block=256. Grid=(ceil(W/32), H, N*ceil(OC/8)). This is a great kernel design for 1x1 conv. For 3x3 depthwise: - Block=(32, 8) where x=w, y=oc (but oc=C_out, and groups=C_out usually for depthwise so oc corresponds to input c). - Grid=(ceil(W/32), H, N*ceil(C/8)). - Each thread computes output[n, c, h, w] by reading 3x3 input window. - In a warp (same c, different w): input accesses are coalesced if w are consecutive. For kw=0, reads input[n,c,h*stride+kh,w*stride+kw]. Since w varies and c,h,n are same, addresses are consecutive -> coalesced. Perfect. - Weight[9] is uniform per warp. Let me prototype the 1x1 kernel: ```cpp __global__ void conv1x1_relu6_kernel( const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out, int relu6_flag) { int w = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int n = blockIdx.z / ((C_out + blockDim.y - 1) / blockDim.y); int oc_block = blockIdx.z % ((C_out + blockDim.y - 1) / blockDim.y); int oc = oc_block * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; if (w >= W || h >= H || n >= N || oc >= C_out) return; float acc = bias[oc]; for (int ic = 0; ic < C_in; ++ic) { acc += in[((n * C_in + ic) * H + h) * W + w] * weight[oc * C_in + ic]; } if (relu6_flag) { acc = fmaxf(acc, 0.0f); acc = fminf(acc, 6.0f); } out[((n * C_out + oc) * H + h) * W + w] = acc; } ``` Wait: blockDim.y is block size in y dimension. `threadIdx.y` ranges from 0 to blockDim.y-1. If blockDim.y=8, then oc_block * 8 + threadIdx.y. But in a warp (32 threads), if blockDim.x=32, then warps are naturally formed with threadIdx.x varying and threadIdx.y constant. Since blockDim=32x8, thread IDs are threadIdx.y * 32 + threadIdx.x. So threads 0..31 have threadIdx.y=0, threadIdx.x=0..31 -> same oc, different w. Perfect! Threads 32..63 have threadIdx.y=1, same as above. So each warp has uniform oc, varying w. Weight uniform, input coalesced. Similarly for 3x3 depthwise: ```cpp template <int K> __global__ void conv_depthwise_relu6_kernel( const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int stride, int pad, int relu6_flag) { int w = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int n = blockIdx.z / ((C + blockDim.y - 1) / blockDim.y); int c_block = blockIdx.z % ((C + blockDim.y - 1) / blockDim.y); int c = c_block * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; if (w >= W_out || h >= H_out || n >= N || c >= C) return; float acc = bias[c]; for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int ih = h * stride + kh - pad; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < K; ++kw) { int iw = w * stride + kw - pad; if (iw < 0 || iw >= W) continue; acc += in[((n * C + c) * H + ih) * W + iw] * weight[((c) * K + kh) * K + kw]; } } if (relu6_flag) { acc = fmaxf(acc, 0.0f); acc = fminf(acc, 6.0f); } out[((n * C + c) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = acc; } ``` Weight indexing for depthwise: weight shape is [C, 1, K, K] or [C_out, C_in/groups, K, K] = [C, 1, K, K]. So weight[c, 0, kh, kw]. We can fold the 1. So weight_idx = ((c * 1 + 0) * K + kh) * K + kw = (c * K + kh) * K + kw. Actually, in our fused weights, I will pre-process to [C_out, K, K] for depthwise. Or just keep [C_out, 1, K, K] and index appropriately. For simplicity in the kernel, I'll pass weight reshaped to [C_out, K*K] or [C_out, K, K]. Since C++ code creates the tensor, I can reshape it. Wait, the weight tensors in Python are already shaped [OC, IC/groups, K, K]. For depthwise, IC/groups = 1. So I can use `weight.data_ptr<float>()` and index with [((oc * (C_in/groups)) + ic_per_group) * K * K + kh * K + kw]. Since ic_per_group is 0 for depthwise, and C_in/groups is c_per_group. But in our 1x1 kernel, the weight is [OC, C_in, 1, 1], so `weight[oc * C_in + ic]` works. For the 3x3 depthwise kernel with groups, C_in = C_out = C, and c_per_group = C/groups. Wait, if groups=C, then c_per_group = C/C = 1. The weight shape is [C, 1, 3, 3]. We just need `weight[(c * 1) * 9 + kh*3 + kw]`. Yes. Actually, let me keep weight in C++ as the original shape and compute indices directly. For 1x1 conv with groups=1, groups info not needed. For depthwise, groups=C. For my C++ code, I can just have a single generic conv kernel that handles all cases, or dispatch based on params. Actually, the `fused_conv2d_kernel_t` in my earlier attempt is generic. The issue is performance. But I can write two optimized kernels for the 1x1 and 3x3 depthwise case, and for the first 3x3 standard conv use at::conv2d. Or I can write an optimized generic kernel for 3x3 with constant unrolling. Let me write the C++ code now with: 1. Custom 1x1 kernel (with block=(32, oc_tile)) 2. Custom 3x3 kernel with loop unroll and optimal warp layout. 3. Avg pool kernel. 4. The C++ forward function dispatches to these. Wait, for 3x3 standard conv (groups=1), my earlier generic kernel can still work, but if blockDim.y covers output channels and blockDim.x covers width, then warp has uniform oc, varying w. The inner loop runs over all C_in. Weight access is uniform per warp if oc is uniform. Input access is coalesced if w varies. This is actually also optimal for standard 3x3 conv! Wait, is it? For standard 3x3 conv with groups=1: - Warp has same oc, varying w. - Each thread accesses input[n, c, h*stride+kh, w*stride+kw] for varying w -> coalesced within a warp for fixed c, kh, kw. - The loop is over c from 0 to C_in, and over kh, kw. - Weight[oc, c, kh, kw] is uniform per warp. - So yes, the same kernel layout works great for 3x3 standard conv too! For 3x3 depthwise (groups=C): - The loop over c runs only c_per_group times. For depthwise, c_per_group=1. - So each thread does only 9 MACs. Very fast. - The same grid mapping works. For 3x3 with K=3: - Loop over ic from 0 to C_in/groups, and kh,kw from 0..2. - Grid/Block same. - Each thread computes one output. The only difference is the number of MACs per thread. The kernel: ```cpp template <int K, bool RELU6> __global__ void fused_conv_relu6_kernel( const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out, int H_out, int W_out, int stride, int pad, int groups) { ``` Wait, I need groups. For standard conv, groups=1. For depthwise, groups=C_in=C_out. Actually for a generic conv: - g = oc / (C_out/groups) Wait, `groups` is the number of groups. `C_out_per_group = C_out / groups` `C_in_per_group = C_in / groups` For each output channel oc, group_id = oc / C_out_per_group. Then ic iterates from 0 to C_in_per_group - 1. `c = group_id * C_in_per_group + ic` Weight index: ((oc * C_in_per_group + ic) * K + kh) * K + kw. Yes. So the kernel code: ```cpp template <int K, bool RELU6> __global__ void fused_conv_relu6_kernel( const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out, int H_out, int W_out, int stride, int pad, int groups) { int w = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int n = blockIdx.z / ((C_out + blockDim.y - 1) / blockDim.y); int oc_block = blockIdx.z % ((C_out + blockDim.y - 1) / blockDim.y); int oc = oc_block * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; if (w >= W_out || h >= H_out || n >= N || oc >= C_out) return; float acc = bias[oc]; int c_out_per_group = C_out / groups; int c_in_per_group = C_in / groups; int g = oc / c_out_per_group; for (int ic = 0; ic < c_in_per_group; ++ic) { int c = g * c_in_per_group + ic; float w_val; // weight cache #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int ih = h * stride + kh - pad; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H) continue; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < K; ++kw) { int iw = w * stride + kw - pad; if (iw < 0 || iw >= W) continue; float val = in[((n * C_in + c) * H + ih) * W + iw]; float wt = weight[((oc * c_in_per_group + ic) * K + kh) * K + kw]; acc += val * wt; } } } if (RELU6) { acc = fmaxf(acc, 0.0f); acc = fminf(acc, 6.0f); } out[((n * C_out + oc) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = acc; } ``` The block size: I'll use `dim3 block(32, 8);` - but what if C_out is less than 8? Then some threads are masked by the `if (oc >= C_out)` check. That's fine. Wait, one issue: `blockDim.y` is 8, but `oc_block` depends on `(C_out + blockDim.y - 1) / blockDim.y`. However, `blockDim.y` in the kernel is fixed at 8. In the C++ host code, I need to launch with `dim3 block(32, 8)`. Yes. But what about C_out=1 or 2? Still ok. `oc_block` ranges from 0 to ceil(C_out/8)-1. `threadIdx.y` goes 0..7. `oc` = oc_block*8 + threadIdx.y. For C_out=1, only oc=0 passes. One more thing: for the initial conv (3x3, C_in=3, C_out=32, groups=1, H=W=224, stride=2, pad=1): - H_out=112, W_out=112. - grid.x = ceil(112/32) = 4. - grid.y = 112. - grid.z = 10 * ceil(32/8) = 10*4 = 40. - Total blocks = 4*112*40 = 17920. - Each block = 256 threads. - Each thread does C_in=3, K=3, so 27 MACs. Total work = 10*32*112*112*27 = 10.8B MACs? Wait, 10*32*112*112=4M outputs, each 27 MACs = 108M MACs. At ~20 TFLOPS, 0.005 ms. Memory bandwidth: input 10*3*224*224*4=6MB, weight 32*3*9*4=3.5KB, output 10*32*112*112*4=16MB. Total ~22MB. At 1TB/s, 0.022 ms. So ~0.03 ms. Very fast. For the heaviest layers, like projection 1x1 from 320->1280 at 7x7: - N=10, C_in=320, C_out=1280, H=W=7. - grid.x=1, grid.y=7, grid.z=10*160=1600. - Each thread does 320 MACs. - Total outputs = 10*1280*7*7 = 627k. Total MACs = 200M. - Runtime maybe 0.1-0.2 ms. For expansion 1x1 in some blocks: e.g. from 96 to 576 at 14x14: - grid.x=1, grid.y=14, grid.z=10*72=720. - each thread 96 MACs. total outputs=10*576*14*14=1.13M. total MACs=108M. ~0.1ms. Overall, this custom kernel approach might be competitive with cuDNN, especially since we fuse ReLU6 directly. But there's a concern: comparing to cuDNN, will this naive C loop be slower? cuDNN for 1x1 uses GEMM with cuBLAS, which is highly optimized. For a 1x1 conv with out=10*1280*7*7=627k and in=320, this is a GEMM of (627k, 320) x (320, 1280) = 627k x 1280. cuBLAS handles this with tensor cores maybe. This is very fast, maybe 0.02-0.05 ms. Our kernel: each thread 320 MACs = 320 FLOPs. 256 threads/block = 82k FLOPs/block. With 1600 blocks = 131M FLOPs. At 20 TFLOPS, 0.006ms ideal. But memory bandwidth for weights: 1280*320*4 = 1.6MB. Input: 10*320*7*7*4=0.6MB. Output: 10*1280*7*7*4=2.5MB. Total ~5MB. At 1TB/s, 0.005ms. So theoretically it could be as fast as 0.02ms. But cache efficiency matters. However, cuDNN likely reaches near-peak for such GEMM-like operations, whereas our kernel might only reach ~20-50% due to non-optimal scheduling. But it's not necessarily 5x slower. And remember: the reference has 50+ layers with Python overhead. If our total time per layer is similar or slightly slower, but we avoid Python overhead and fuse ReLU6, we could still win. But if we're significantly slower on key layers, we lose. Is there a way to quantitatively know? I can only test. But I realize one issue: the weights for 1x1 conv are of shape [1280, 320, 1, 1]. In our kernel, weight access is `weight[((oc * c_in_per_group + ic) * K + kh) * K + kw]`. For K=1, this is `weight[oc * C_in + ic]`. Since in a warp, oc is uniform, but ic varies across the loop. So within a warp, all threads access the same weight values but at different ic in different loop iterations. Actually, in the loop, ic goes from 0 to C_in-1. At iteration ic, all threads in the warp access `weight[oc * C_in + ic]`, which is the same address. This is a broadcast, perfectly cached. Input access: `in[((n * C_in + c) * H + ih) * W + iw]`. At iteration ic=c, threads in warp access different w (consecutive), same n,c,h. So they access `base + w`, which is coalesced. Great! So our 1x1 kernel should have excellent memory patterns. For 3x3 depthwise: loop over ic is only 1 iteration. kh,kw loops 9 iterations. At each (kh,kw), threads access different w - coalesced. Weight is uniform per warp. Also great. So the kernel design is actually very solid. It might be quite close to optimal for direct convolution and comparable to cuDNN for these specific MobileNetV2 shapes! For depthwise: groups=hidden_dim (e.g. 96). c_out_per_group = 96/96 = 1. c_in_per_group = 96/96 = 1. g = oc / 1 = oc. ic in [0,0], c = oc * 1 + 0 = oc. Correct. Weight idx: ((oc * 1 + 0) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw. Weight[oc, 0, kh, kw]. Linear index: ((oc * 1 + 0) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw. Correct. Now the pooling: after the last conv (H=7, W=7, C=1280), we do adaptive avg pool to 1x1. My pool kernel: one thread per (n,c). Computes mean over H*W. ```cpp __global__ void adaptive_avg_pool_kernel( const float* __restrict__ in, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C, int H, int W) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C; if (idx >= total) return; float sum = 0.0f; int base = idx * H * W; int hw = H * W; for (int i = 0; i < hw; ++i) { sum += in[base + i]; } out[idx] = sum / float(hw); } ``` Grid/Block: block=256, grid=(total+255)/256. This is correct. For the linear layer, `at::addmm(linear_bias, pooled, linear_weight.t())`. `linear_bias` is [num_classes]. `pooled` is [N, last_channel]. `linear_weight` is [num_classes, last_channel] (from nn.Linear). `linear_weight.t()` is [last_channel, num_classes]. `at::addmm` computes `beta*C + alpha*(A @ B)`. Here we want `pooled @ W^T + bias`. `at::addmm(linear_bias, pooled, linear_weight.t())` is `linear_bias + pooled @ linear_weight.t()`. `linear_bias` is 1D [num_classes], it will be broadcast to [N, num_classes]. reasoning
The compile tool passes the code to a dedicated runner that imports it; the loader runs in a separate environment, so there might be a mismatch with the extension library path. I should avoid inline compilation and instead use precompiled (torch-libraries-based) or write the kernel with CUDAExtension but that requires building; load_inline compiled the extension but loading failed. Maybe there's a linker error against a newer libstdc++. That might be due to mixing conda GCC with system libstdc++. We can ask load_inline to compile with `extra_ldflags` or compile with older ABI? Or maybe avoid C++ extension entirely and write a triton kernel or use `torch.compile` / `cudnn`? The challenge says "custom CUDA kernel", but using Triton is also a valid custom GPU kernel. If C++ extension loading is broken due to system libraries, maybe Triton works. Let me try Triton. I'll write a Triton based kernel for conv-style fused operations? For MobileNetV2, Triton can be used to write fused kernels for some layers, but implementing a full conv net with Triton from scratch would be massive. Alternative: use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` but with careful flags to avoid ABI mismatch? Could add `-D_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=0` in extra_cflags / extra_cuda_cflags to force old ABI. Let me try that first. Also the error says `libstdc++.so.6: version CXXABI_1.3.15' not found`. That means the extension was compiled with newer libstdc++ (from conda) but loaded with system old libstdc++. Maybe set `LD_LIBRARY_PATH`? Not possible. Let's try adding `extra_cflags=['-D_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=0']` and maybe `extra_ldflags=['-D_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=0']` to load_inline. Or maybe use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load` with `extra_include_paths`... Probably not worth. Triton is a good fallback. We can write a kernel that uses Triton for each layer? MobileNetV2 has many convs, but we could use triton for elementwise operations and maybe reduce memory traffic. However, the main cost is depthwise and pointwise convs; cuDNN is very optimized for these. To be faster than PyTorch reference, we need to fuse BN and ReLU, and possibly skip intermediate allocations. Writing a giant kernel for all layers is too large. But we could fuse each conv + BN + ReLU6 using Triton? Actually, Triton has a matmul tutorial but conv is trickier. It's possible to write a simple pointwise (1x1) conv kernel using reduction over C. Depthwise 3x3 could be written with loops. Given we have an H100 with massive compute and bandwidth, the PyTorch reference is likely memory-bound due to many small kernels and intermediate allocations. A fused kernel that processes all layers sequentially in one CUDA kernel launch with reduced memory traffic could be much faster. But implementing a full MobileNetV2 as a single CUDA kernel is massive but doable: unroll all layers in host Python, but each layer is a separate kernel launch? We can at least fuse conv+bn+relu into one kernel per layer using a simple CUDA kernel, reducing overhead. However the reference already uses cuDNN which is highly optimized. For small channel counts, cuDNN overhead might dominate. A custom simple conv kernel could have less overhead but might be slower for large convolutions. Given MobileNetV2 on H100, the inference time with batch_size=10 might be ~0.2ms or so. Overhead of many kernel launches matters. Alternative approach: Use `torch.compile` with `mode='reduce-overhead'` or fullgraph. That would likely speed up significantly due to fusion. But the challenge requires a custom CUDA kernel. However torch.compile might generate Triton kernels; but the evaluation probably compares ModelNew against plain PyTorch Model. If ModelNew uses torch.compile, is that allowed? The instructions say "Write a custom CUDA kernel that replaces a PyTorch reference, producing numerically equivalent output faster than the reference. Use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` to compile and bind CUDA source at module load time... Do not submit raw CUDA C..." So it wants a load_inline extension. The compilation issue may just be with the inline loader and can be fixed. Let's add abi flag.

A typical fix in KernelBench-like environments where `load_inline` is used is to avoid custom C++ extensions altogether and just do the forward pass with optimized PyTorch operations (e.g., fused Conv+BN, torch.compile, torch.jit.script, etc.), but the task explicitly requests a custom CUDA kernel. Another approach: instead of `load_inline`, use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_source` or write a custom CUDA kernel with pybind11 proper, but that risks hitting the same ABI issue. Another possibility: the `.so` is compiled with a different ABI setting because PyTorch itself was compiled with the new ABI. We have to use the compiler/linker flags to force linking against the old ABI and/or avoid newer symbols. Possible ideas: - Build with `-static-libstdc++`. This embeds libstdc++ into the `.so`, avoids relying on system libstdc++. That should bypass the ABI version mismatch. Let me try that. Actually, this is a known trick: adding `-static-libstdc++` to linker flags (extra_ldflags) can resolve this because the needed C++ symbols get embedded in the shared library. Another option could be to set `LD_PRELOAD=/opt/conda/lib/libstdc++.so.6`, but we can't easily do that from within Python. Let me try using `extra_ldflags=["-static-libstdc++"]` or maybe `extra_cflags=["-static-libstdc++"]`.
